Aliko Dangote: Building Africa's industrial future from the ground up | Podcast | In Good Company

Norges Bank Investment Management(112.0K)·49:25·120.8K views·2026-05-13
Nicolai TangenCEO, Norges Bank Investment Management Aliko DangoteFounder and CEO, Dangote Group
TL;DRAliko Dangote is spearheading Africa’s transition toward industrial self-sufficiency through massive infrastructure projects—including the world’s largest oil refinery—with a target of reaching $100 billion in annual revenue and $30 billion in EBITDA by 2030.
Legacy of...

TL;DR

Aliko Dangote is spearheading Africa’s transition toward industrial self-sufficiency through massive infrastructure projects—including the world’s largest oil refinery—with a target of reaching $100 billion in annual revenue and $30 billion in EBITDA by 2030.

Entrepreneurial Philosophy and Early Origins

  • Legacy of Trading: Dangote began his career in 1978 as a cement trader in Lagos, following in the footsteps of his grandfather, Sanusi Dantata, once the richest man in West Africa.
  • The Discipline of Work: He attributes his success to extreme discipline and a "business as hobby" mindset.
      • Schedule: Typically works from 5:30 AM until late at night, six or seven days a week.
      • Sacrifice: Sold luxury properties in the US and UK to eliminate distractions and remain fully committed to his operations in Nigeria.
  • Strategic Focus: His core strategy is "backward integration"—moving from trading imported goods to manufacturing them locally to meet essential human needs (cement, sugar, salt, and fuel).

The $20 Billion Refinery: A Continental Game-Changer

  • Project Scale: Dangote invested $20 billion to build the world’s largest single-train oil refinery, a project many deemed impossible.
      • Workforce: At its peak, the project employed 67,000 people.
      • Self-Reliance: Because local infrastructure was insufficient, the group built its own port, roads, and a water treatment facility capable of processing 440 million liters per day.
  • Overcoming Resistance: Dangote faced significant opposition from an "oil mafia"—shippers and traders who benefited from the $10 billion annual subsidy system previously used for imported fuel.
  • Economic Impact: The refinery eliminates the absurdity of Africa’s largest oil producer exporting crude and importing refined products, which has historically drained foreign reserves.

Financial Strategy and 2030 Targets

  • Revenue Goals: The group aims to scale from current levels to $100 billion in revenue by 2030, with a projected market valuation of over $200 billion.
  • De-risking for Investors: To attract foreign capital, the group is transitioning to an export-heavy model.
      • Dollar Dividends: 80% of revenue is targeted to be in USD, allowing the group to pay dividends in dollars and eliminate the foreign exchange risks associated with the Naira.
  • Reinvestment Policy: Historically, the parent company (Dangote Industries) has never paid a dividend to Aliko personally, instead reinvesting 100% of profits back into African expansion.

Africa’s Industrial Future and Geopolitics

  • The Demographic Dividend: With 70% of Africa’s population under the age of 30, Dangote views the continent as the world's next great growth engine.
  • Foreign Investment Shift: Dangote notes that China has dominated African investment because they "put their balance sheet on the table" via Sinosure and long-term credit. However, he sees renewed interest from US and Japanese institutions.
  • Infrastructure for Tax Credits: To mitigate government policy risks, the group utilizes a policy where it builds public roads (over $3 billion invested) in exchange for tax credits, ensuring infrastructure exists to support their industrial output.
  • Sustainability: The group is converting 90% of its massive truck fleet to Compressed Natural Gas (CNG) and investing in solar and wind power to address climate concerns while expanding cement production.

Notable Quotes

"My business is actually my hobby. I take my business as a hobby because if I take it as a job, I won't work this hard." — Aliko Dangote
"Luckily for us, we didn't know what we were building. Because if I knew... I would have actually chickened out. It's like you've started swimming across the ocean; when you get to the middle, the tide is bad, so you have to work forward." — Aliko Dangote
"Foreign investors are only attracted by domestic investors. We have to show people the potentials of Africa by investing ourselves." — Aliko Dangote
"My legacy will be that I'm one of the ones that actually pioneered the industrialization of Africa. To make sure that Africa produces what we consume." — Aliko Dangote
Hi everybody, I'm Nicolai Tangen, the CEO of the Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund, and today I have a real pleasure sitting down with Aliko Dangote. Aliko is the founder and the CEO of the Dangote Group, Africa's largest industrial conglomerate. Nearly 50 years ago, he started with a small trading firm in Nigeria, and today his empire stretches across the continent. He has built what many said was impossible: a large-scale African industry from scratch. Warm welcome. Thank you very much, Nicolai. It's a pleasure. It's great to be here. I started as a trading company in 1978. At that time, I started first with cement. I was getting four or five trucks and trading them there in Lagos. And then later on, I got into fish, into rice, and into sugar. We continued to expand. My first import of sugar was actually in 1980. Everything was under import license, so I got a license. My family had been into trading for several generations from the maternal side. My mom's grandfather, they called him Alhassan Dantata. He was actually even trading in Nigeria and Ghana. He died in 1955 before I was born, but he was the richest West African during his time. And of course, my own late grandfather Sanusi Dantata was actually the richest Nigerian too at some point. I grew up with my grandfather because my father died when I was just about eight years old. I never actually stayed with my father because when I was born, I was the first grandson. In our tradition, the first grandchild goes to stay with the grandparents. My late sister happened to be the first grandchild, so they took her away. And then the next was me, a boy, so they also took me away. Both me and my sister grew up with my own grandparents. It was my grandfather who taught me how to trade. He sent me to school, but the key first of all is actually discipline. You have to be very, very disciplined. You have to know what you are trading. It's not that you'll be a jack of all trades; no. You must choose what you really want to do for a life and then you pick up and concentrate on that. Pleasure and business, they don't mix at all. That is the kind of discipline that I got. My grandfather used to start working from six in the morning and he'll work till late. He had a very simple life. Some days he'd drive himself. That is how simple he was. He was a very disciplined, honest person, and I actually took a lot of things from him. First of all, I learned to be very honest and to make sure that you are fair to everybody. I also learned to be generous. He was very generous. To tell you how generous he was, he gave out all his assets and money before he passed. And then also in terms of business, he has always been very, very fair. When people say that business and pleasure don't mix, it doesn't mean I don't like what I do. I love what I do. When you look at the business today, people see that I work so hard because my business is actually my hobby. I take my business as a hobby. If I took it as a job, I wouldn't work this hard. I go to bed maybe sometimes at 2:00 or 2:30, but I wake up around 5:30 in the morning. I go to the gym, and by 8:15, I'm done. Then I go to my office. I work as hard as any staff. I'm close to 70 years old; I turned 69 just about three weeks ago. I think that discipline is good because I don't want to get distracted. For example, when I decided to go into industry, I sold all my properties in the US. I had two houses in the US, big mansions, and I had a house in the UK. I wanted to really sit in Nigeria and concentrate. Sometimes when you own a holiday home anywhere, you have to create that time to go and use that property. Now my life is very simple. Wherever I go, I use hotels and I pay. When I leave, nobody will call me and say I have a burst pipe or something is wrong. I'm committed to what I do. I just don't do things off-hand; I always create a vision. It's just like now we created a Vision 2030 so that I know I have a target to meet. I just don't do business on a normal basis. All my businesses are targeted. I first of all look at what we need as people. What is it that we're supposed to be producing and we're instead importing? So we do what you call backward integration, which means what we are importing now, we produce it ourselves. We produce what we need. We are now producing things that when you wake up as a human being, every morning you must use part of what we produce. That’s like cement and sugar. I exited the flour mill and textile business because of the foreign exchange challenges. But today we are very good in terms of exports. Part of what we're announcing is that when you invest in any of our businesses going forward—in the cement, the refinery, the petrochemical, the fertilizer, or the port—we guarantee to pay you dividends in dollars because we are very well into exports. Eighty percent of our revenue will be in dollars. So that is why we are now saying that you can have a choice. If you want locals, we can pay you in Naira. If you don't want Naira, you don't want to de-risk. Most foreign investors find that when they get their dividend, it's difficult to get money to buy and remit your money. But we have addressed that issue. We are saying, "Look, invest." From the inception of Dangote Industries to date, we have never ever paid any dividend. If we are going to get dividends, we only get them from companies that we listed. But companies that are wholly owned by Dangote Industries, which are not listed, we never ever take a dime out of the company. We generally reinvest the money back into the business. A major decision was to go into cement in trying to save a lot of African countries because they were struggling. Cement was selling for $250 a ton in countries like Zambia and Congo Brazzaville. In Nigeria, you had to pay and wait for three months. So we realized that people who were in cement were not really serious about making cement available. We now have invested heavily and are in 14 countries. In Africa, we have a lot of countries that produce oil but they don't refine it. So they export the crude oil and then they import the products. Importation costs a lot of money and goes deep into our own reserves. When I looked at Nigeria, I saw we've been having fuel queues for 52 years. Sometimes people stayed at fuel stations during Christmas for two days trying to buy fuel for a country that produces oil. The government refineries were all destroyed and were not doing well. So I said, okay fine, let me take this bold decision. I decided that I'm going to make a refinery. I wanted to make the biggest ever in the world—50% more than the biggest. It was a 20 billion dollar investment. We launched the project in 2013 and we had issues with the land for five years. One land took three and a half years, and the other land took one and a half years where we were not able to get even access to it. All this time, we've been blocked by what you call the mafia in the oil business to make sure that we don't come and address these issues. But we were not deterred at all. We were focused and we knew what we were doing. When we started, the exchange rate of Naira was 156. It even got up to 1,900. But we still went ahead. We had to build our own port because there was no port in the country that could take that heavy equipment. One piece was 3,000 tons. We have another which is the regenerator. Then we have another piece which is the crude distillation unit at 2,700 tons. We have almost about 30 of these equipments and we built the refinery importing most of them in modular forms. We had to build roads and we had to do water. Water is 440 million liters of treated water. Our water department alone is more than 30 hectares in size. 67,000 people worked on the building of the refinery and it took us six years. Luckily for us, we didn't know what we were building. Because if I knew and was faced with the plan and the drawings all at once, I wouldn't have built this refinery. I would have actually chickened out. When you start swimming across the ocean and get to the middle, you realize the tide is bad. You can't go forward or backward, so you have to work forward. That is what we did. We just kept working and believing that we would deliver. We got a lot of support. We were meant to fund most of this from our internally generated funds, but because of the devaluation, we had to rely on Afreximbank, African Finance Corporation, Zenith Bank, Access Bank, UBA, and a couple of some of the local banks. We also have a very good relationship with Standard Bank of South Africa and, at the beginning, Standard Chartered Bank of the UK. When we finished the refinery, things turned out to be much more than our expectations. The effect on our businesses is actually more of a beneficiary than a downside. Fertilizer is in very high demand. In February, before the Middle Eastern crisis, we were selling a ton for about $400. Today we are selling a ton of fertilizer for $850 and we are actually oversold. In plastics, polypropylene has moved from $900 up to about $3,000 in the UK. If not because of the polypropylene we're producing today, all the plastic industries in Nigeria would have shut down because there's no way you can even get it. Our aviation fuel is oversold until the middle of July and we're producing 20 million liters a day. We source about 56% of our crude from Nigeria. We also buy from Angola, Libya, and the US. At one point we were doing almost about seven to eight cargoes of WTI from the US, but we are getting more of Nigeria's crude now. We have to buy 21 cargoes every month. We are more than doubling the refinery in the next 30 months to 1.4 million, which is huge. The people who were actually benefiting from the status quo were working against us. Nigeria was giving almost 10 billion dollars every year as a subsidy. Shippers and traders were making tons of money buying crude and sending us products. There are also local people who were getting allocations and making billions of Naira because it was subsidized. These people do not want us to settle down because we have displaced them. There are a couple of countries that say that with what has happened in the Middle East, they don't want to rely on the supply from there anymore. They need their own refinery. Uganda, Tanzania, Kenya, Rwanda, and Ethiopia could be served. We are going to build. As a group, we have $45 billion to spend. We are also doing LNG in Nigeria and building gas infrastructure to remove all the gas we are flaring in the southern and eastern parts to bring it to the west. We are setting up an LNG plant for about 12 million tons. Each train will be about 6 million. We are piping it and we have the gas treatment plant. Nigerian gas is associated gas, so you have to treat it before you can regasify it. We are looking at our income stream and what the EBITDA will be between 2026 and 2030. We are coming up with selling part of the business, getting more investors into the business, and accelerating. Cement is going to 100 million tons. In cement, we don't even need much money; the cash generation is very liquid. We'll be able to fund this $45 billion, which will eventually take us to $100 billion of revenue. Our target is to get to $100 billion by 2030 with a market valuation of maybe more than $200-250 billion. Last year our EBITDA was $3 billion, but the target by 2030 is to be at over $30 billion of EBITDA. If you think big, you grow big. When you think small, you don't grow at all. You have to know the business you are going into in and out. Myself and any of the top 10 staff can tell you the process of each part of our business if you wake us late at night. We don't overstretch because we make sure that our funding is handled with no sweat. Financial institutions believe in us because we have a history of delivery. They know their money is not going to be at risk. We delivered a refinery that has never been built with petrochemicals. We managed 67,000 workers at the site during COVID in a very difficult environment and we were able to deliver. The refinery has been tested and we have processed crude at 661,000 barrels per day. Financial institutions see that if it is Dangote doing the project, they are there to back us. The biggest risk for us is actually if there is a civil war, which is not in the offing at all. The other biggest risk is government inconsistencies in policies. We are addressing that because for our refinery, the national oil company already owns 7.25% and they are trying to buy more. We want to spread it and have everybody be part of it. Another challenge was infrastructure. We are building roads worth more than $3 billion. Because our companies are doing well, our taxes are high. There is a government policy where they allow us to defray what we have invested in the roads over a period of three years. We don't really wait for the government to give us that infrastructure; we build it ourselves to absorb the kind of businesses we are doing. We are not really depending on the government. Even in America, if a leader wakes up on the wrong side of the bed, he can bring a regulation that helps or slows you down. African governments believe that the only way to create jobs is through the private sector because the government is already full to capacity. When you give good investment policies, it makes the governments partners. Even if I don't make money, I have generated revenue where the government collects VAT and minimum taxation. In our cement business, the government is making more money than us because of the various taxes that we pay. It is a partnership where the government gives good regulation and we don't abuse the system. Foreign investors are sometimes baffled that Africans are not investing in our own economies. But foreign investors are only attracted by domestic investors. That is what we are doing today because we are investing heavily. We are in a very big discussion with ADNOC for a serious partnership in fertilizer, oil, and infrastructure. We are also building the biggest, deepest port in Africa near Lagos. The government realizes that to deliver on electoral promises, they have to work with the private sector. If the government disrupts our business, they'll be the first ones to suffer. Doing business across Africa is not very difficult because it depends on the culture you create in your organization. If you have a solid organization and culturally trained staff, it is as good as operating anywhere in the world. We don't just pick people from the street; we have an academy and graduate training. We employ the best in universities. We give them a choice: if you are happy, stay with us, but if you have a better offer from Shell or any other company, you can go there. We have also trained over 200 Africans through the Young Global Leaders in partnership with the World Economic Forum. The World Economic Forum only gives Africans five slots, so I said no, increase it to 30 and the Dangote Foundation will pay the bill. It costs us about 650,000 to 700,000 Swiss francs every year. We pick people below 40, some of whom are ministers, so they can join these global leaders. Africa will have 1.6 billion young working staff by 2030. The future is Africa. We need heavy investment in infrastructure and education. Governments are putting an average of 15% to 20% of their budget into education because we have to up-skill. Promising countries include Nigeria, Ethiopia, Kenya, Tanzania, Rwanda, Egypt, Algeria, Ghana, and Cote d'Ivoire. Even in Guinea, people are realizing Africa is open for business. China has dominated business in Africa because of the absence of others. They are making money and dominating the landscape. African governments don't joke with China because China puts their balance sheet on the table. For cement projects, they give supplier's credit backed by their insurance company, Sinosure. Sinosure has invested about $1.2 trillion in supporting their companies to go abroad and sell technology. If I go to Italy for a $500 million power plant, they might ask for a check, but the Chinese will say just give me 20% and the rest is five years of financing. Obviously, you take the Chinese one because it means I can do more. I need to leverage the business to get to where I want to be. US policy is also changing. The Development Finance Corporation has about $208 billion and they are very hungry for infrastructure and ports. I told a delegation from Japan that they have been missing for a long time. If they come, they must come with their balance sheet on the table because we have choices. We need that support to leapfrog to the next level. Inequality must be addressed, and African governments are working toward that. Regarding climate, we are not the biggest polluters; we are less than 6% of global pollution. In cement today, it is not like before. The process we have is more stringent than that of Europe. We are using robotic machines and the dust emission is very low. We are using alternative fuel to cut down on pollution. The biggest polluters are shipping companies, so we are stopping almost 500 ships from picking up crude to go and refine and bring back products. Ninety percent of our trucks are all CNG; we are not using diesel. We are also doing solar and wind because we have massive land. We are trying to provide what we need while simultaneously bringing climate issues into place. The key to patience is having good people that you are leading. Sometimes you have to be very patient for people to pick up and prepare themselves for leadership. I make sure that when I'm hiring people, I always hire people who are more intelligent than me. In interviews, we don't just use Zoom; we sit down to see if there is chemistry. To tell you the truth, I'm more aggressive now than when I was younger. I've seen a lot of opportunities that I never knew existed. I have never ever thought of my company being 10% of where we are today, and we still have a lot of opportunities to go. Look at Singapore; they don't have natural resources but they created something out of nothing. Africa controls about 60% of the world's critical minerals. We have the most arable land in the world and landmass. We are not actually processing any of our minerals yet. European countries are now running to see where they can get energy from. Nigeria has more gas than oil. We can be one of the biggest suppliers of LNG in the world. We want to do a gas pipeline taking gas all the way from Nigeria up to Spain by the coast. We are already in Ghana, so the next phase will be Cote d'Ivoire, Mauritania, and Senegal. Africa will be a supplier of most of these critical items. Legacy is what is driving me now; it's not about being the richest because I don't have many needs. In my religion, Islam, I cannot just do a giving pledge unless the inheritors agree, but I have given one-third of my wealth to the foundation. If anything happens to me, one-third automatically goes there. What drives me most is that we have a lot of needs in Africa. Corporations like us are supposed to provide leadership in transforming the economies. My legacy will be that I pioneered the industrialization of Africa. My focus is to make sure Africa produces what we consume. We want to have one common market. I have a group called the African Renaissance with 54 of us who meet in Rwanda. Nobody can transform our continent but us, we Africans. We are trying to mobilize entrepreneurs to join us at different levels. The foundation did an endowment of $1.25 billion in 2014. We work in partnership with Bill Gates to eradicate polio and work on primary healthcare and nutrition. In Nigeria, we have 10 million kids out of school, so we are investing heavily in education. As we grow more, we'll keep funding the foundation for poverty alleviation and empowerment. To relax, I take my grandkids to the beach. We just go there and play. I am also a big Arsenal fan. I almost bought the team, but I had to make a choice. I was facing the challenge of making sure the refinery, fertilizer, and petrochemical projects reached completion. Should I put $2 billion in Arsenal and allow the business to suffer? I decided to remain a big supporter instead. I watch their games and I wear the T-shirt every time they are playing. I have one signed by the captain. It is better for me to remain as a fan and continue to fund my own business. My advice to young African people is to work hard and believe that the future is greater than the current situation. Africa is a promised land and they should stay at home to collectively build our continent.

Amazon's Durability | Stratechery by Ben Thompson

Stratechery(14.3K)·19:51·692 views·2026-05-13
Ben ThompsonAuthor, Stratechery
TL;DRAmazon’s long-term strategy of building internal "primitives"—from logistics and custom silicon to satellite networks—and later selling them as third-party services creates a structural cost advantage and a durable moat that is increasingly well-positioned for the AI inference market.

TL;DR

Amazon’s long-term strategy of building internal "primitives"—from logistics and custom silicon to satellite networks—and later selling them as third-party services creates a structural cost advantage and a durable moat that is increasingly well-positioned for the AI inference market.

The Evolution of the "Amazon Tax" Strategy

Amazon operates on decade-long timeframes, transforming its own internal requirements into high-margin capital assets sold to third parties.

  • Amazon Supply Chain Services (ASCS): This newly unveiled suite consolidates air/ocean freight, trucking, and last-mile delivery into a single package for external companies like Procter & Gamble and 3M.
  • The "First and Best Customer" Model: Amazon justifies massive capital expenditures by using its own demand to drive initial development, eventually gaining leverage by selling those same services to competitors (e.g., AWS, Fulfillment by Amazon).
  • Cost Structuralization: By investing in physical and digital infrastructure, Amazon converts marginal costs (labor/shipping) into capital costs (assets/depreciation), which become more efficient at scale.

AI Pivot: From Training to Inference

While Amazon was initially criticized for being ill-prepared for the AI "training" boom, the market shift toward "inference" (running models) favors Amazon’s existing infrastructure.

  • The Shift to Reasoning and Agents: The AI market is moving from simple token generation to "reasoning models" (O1 moment) and "functional agents" (Opus 4.5 moment). These workloads are highly CPU-dependent and require massive memory rather than just chip-to-chip networking.
  • Infrastructure Advantage: Amazon’s Nitro system—a specialized set of chips and software—allows for "disaggregated resource abstraction." This is better suited for the heterogeneous compute needs of AI agents than the tightly networked NVIDIA-only clusters favored by "me-too" clouds.
  • Vertical Silicon Integration: Amazon’s long-term bet on Annapurna Labs (acquired 2015) has produced Trainium and Graviton chips. In a power-constrained environment, the goal is "tokens per watt." Amazon’s ownership of the logic layer allows them to optimize for cost and efficiency in ways that third-party chip buyers cannot.

Strategic Neutrality and Enterprise Trust

Unlike Microsoft and Google, Amazon’s core business is less threatened by the "existential" software risks of AI, allowing it to act as a more neutral platform.

  • Internal vs. External Conflict: Microsoft and Google are under pressure to prioritize their internal AI software (Search, Office) over cloud customers. Amazon’s core business is rooted in physical goods, making it more comfortable devoting the majority of its chips to customer workloads.
  • Model Agnosticism: Through Bedrock, Amazon provides access to multiple frontier models (including Anthropic’s Claude). This flexibility is a key selling point for enterprises that do not want to be locked into a single provider like OpenAI.
  • The Anthropic Hedge: Amazon used its massive cash flow and chip supply to secure a multi-billion dollar investment in Anthropic, ensuring it remains a primary destination for frontier AI models.

Future Horizons: Project Kuiper and Drone Delivery

Amazon is currently laying the groundwork for the next decade of infrastructure, focusing on low-earth orbit (LEO) satellites and autonomous logistics.

  • Project Kuiper (Leo): CEO Andy Jassy compares the satellite service to AWS, noting it is capital-intensive upfront but offers long-term free cash flow. Amazon plans over 20 launches in 2026 and over 30 in 2027.
  • Solving the Marginal Cost Problem: Drone delivery has been a "long slog," but it represents the ultimate transition from human marginal costs to asset depreciation.
  • Synergistic Infrastructure: A future drone network will require reliable, widespread satellite coverage for guidance—meaning Project Kuiper is likely being built with Amazon’s own logistics future as its "first and best customer."
"Amazon, more than any other company, actually operates with decade-long timeframes, consistently making real-world investments at massive scale that... convert their marginal costs into capital costs." — Ben Thompson
"It’s capital-intensive up front... for assets that you get to leverage over a long period of time. So I like the free cash flow and return on invested capital characteristics of [Project Kuiper] in the medium to long term." — Andy Jassy, Amazon CEO
"The vast majority of customers don't interact with GPUs... you're talking to the interface. And so I think that that's going to be true for all accelerator chips, honestly." — Matt Garman, AWS CEO
Amazon’s Durability was published on Tuesday, May 5, 2026. Listen to Podcast. When it comes to the AI soap opera — there is news every day, and the company on top and the bottom seems to shift by the quarter, if not the month. The news that I find most intriguing and instructive this week is about physical goods and logistics. From Bloomberg: Amazon.com Inc. unveiled a suite of logistics services that will let businesses buy its existing freight and distribution offerings as a package, sending shares of rival delivery companies such as FedEx Corp. and United Parcel Service Inc. lower. The world’s largest online retailer on Monday announced Amazon Supply Chain Services, offering other companies access to its “full portfolio” of supply-chain and distribution offerings. The service largely consolidates a package of existing products — air and ocean freight, trucking and last-mile delivery — into a new suite it says companies like Procter & Gamble Co. and 3M Co. are already using. This is a very satisfying announcement for Stratechery, given it’s the culmination of a prediction I made a decade ago in The Amazon Tax. Amazon at that point had two primary businesses, Amazon.com and AWS, and I made the case in that article that they were actually very similar. In both cases, Amazon built primitives that had Amazon itself as their first best customer, justifying and driving initial development. But in both cases, the ultimate play was to sell those primitives to other companies. It was already clear at the time that logistics would follow the same path. “It seems increasingly clear that Amazon intends to repeat the model when it comes to logistics. After experimenting with six planes last year, the company recently leased 20 more to flesh out its private logistics network. This is on top of registering its China subsidiary as an ocean freight forwarder. So how might this play out? Well, start with the fact that Amazon itself would be this logistics network’s first and best customer, just as was the case with AWS. This justifies the massive expenditure necessary to build out a logistics network that competes with UPS, FedEx, et al, and most outlets are framing these moves as a way for Amazon to rein in shipping costs and improve reliability, especially around the holidays. However, I think it is a mistake to think that Amazon will stop there. Just as they have with AWS and e-commerce distribution, I expect the company to offer its logistics network to third parties, which will increase the return to scale, and by extension, deepen Amazon’s eventual moat.” Now, 10 years later, we are here, with the official unveiling of Amazon Supply Chain Services, and I think the timeframe is an important one. Amazon, more than any other company, actually operates with decade-long timeframes, consistently making real-world investments at massive scale that, one, convert their marginal costs into capital costs, and two, gain leverage on those capital costs by selling them to other businesses. This is, by the way, still a story about AI. A Brief History of AWS. Three years ago SemiAnalysis wrote an article entitled Amazon’s Cloud Crisis: How AWS Will Lose The Future Of Computing, and I found it very compelling. First, though, some history (much of which is covered in SemiAnalysis’ article). Amazon not only invented cloud computing, but realized it would be a commodity market. While most people in tech think about building sustainable differentiation that allows you to charge higher prices, thus producing profit, commodity markets work differently. Their sustainable profits come from having structurally cheaper costs. Amazon developed exactly that: first through having the largest scale, giving the company both buying power and also the most leverage on their development costs; and second through genuine innovation. AWS built a specialized system called Nitro, built on their own chips, that offloaded server management, including network management, storage management, hypervisor management, etc., from the expensive Intel and AMD servers that the company sold access to. This let Amazon run that many more virtual machines on a single server, significantly increasing utilization, i.e., delivering a structural cost advantage. Amazon doubled down on their custom chip efforts with Graviton, their ARM processors. Graviton chips, particularly the first few generations, were inferior to Intel or AMD chips, but that didn't mean they were useless. By that time, AWS had expanded from simply being an infrastructure-as-a-service (IaaS) provider to being a platform-as-a-service (PaaS) provider as well. Infrastructure-as-a-service means you provide raw compute, storage, etc., on which customers can run things like operating systems or databases. Platform-as-a-service means you provide that basic functionality as a service. Amazon Relational Database Service, for example, is a fully managed database that customers can access via a set of APIs without having to worry about actually managing the full database themselves, worrying about scaling, or duplication. This, by extension, means that customers don't need to know and don't need to care about the compute infrastructure that undergirds services like RDS, which has long been Graviton. Platform-as-a-service lets Amazon double-dip in terms of profitability. First, AWS could sell platform-as-a-service products at a higher margin than infrastructure-as-a-service products. And second, the company could leverage its own cheaper silicon to serve those products, reducing their costs. Over time Graviton has become more competitive in performance, while still being cheaper, giving Amazon a lower cost compute instance to sell to end users. But even without third-party take-up, the investment in building its own silicon has paid off over time. Training vs. Inference. Fast forward to AI, and SemiAnalysis’ concern was that all of these optimizations left AWS ill-prepared for AI. One big problem was networking. Rather than implement the best networking from Nvidia and/or Broadcom, Amazon is using its own Nitro and Elastic Fabric Adaptor networking. This works well for many workloads, plus it delivers a cost, performance, and security advantage. There are business, cultural, and security reasons why Amazon will not implement other networking. The cultural one is important: Nitro and networking SoCs generally have been Amazon’s biggest cost advantage for years. It's ingrained into their DNA. Even EFA delivers on this too, but they don't see how new workloads are evolving and that a new tier is needed due to the lack of foresight in their internal workload and infrastructure teams. Amazon is making a deliberate choice of not adopting that we believe will bite them in the future. Another was Amazon’s insistence on building its own chips, which are not only inferior to the best NVIDIA chips in terms of performance, but might also lead to them getting fewer NVIDIA chips going forward. At least some other clouds will implement out-of-node NVLink. That’s where the discussion of prioritization now comes in. AI GPUs face tremendous shortages for at least a full year. This is one of the most pivotal times for AI, and it may mark the haves and the have-nots. NVIDIA is a complete monopoly right now. Why would NVIDIA prioritize Amazon for these GPUs, when they know Amazon will move to their in-house chips as quickly as they can for as many compute workloads as they can? Why would NVIDIA ship tons of GPUs to the cloud that is not using any of their networking, thereby reducing their share of wallet? Instead, Nvidia prioritizes the me-too clouds. Amazon does get meaningful volume, but nowhere close to where demand is. Amazon’s H100 GPU shipments relative to public cloud shipments is significantly lower than their share of the public cloud. Those other clouds also can’t satisfy demand, but they get a bigger percentage of the GPUs they ask NVIDIA for, and as such, firms looking for GPUs for training or inference will move to those clouds. NVIDIA is the kingmaker right now, and they are capitalizing on it. They have to spread the balance of power out to prevent compute share from clustering towards Amazon. These concerns were well-founded in the 2023 time-period when that article was written. That was a time when AI, thanks to ChatGPT, had hit the mainstream, but the largest share of compute still went to training. Training required all of the things that Amazon lacked, particularly the ability to network a large number of NVIDIA GPUs together into one coherent system. In such a system, the most important capability was horizontal networking between chips, so that you could update weights during training, a step that needed to happen serially. It was absolutely the case that cloud providers like Microsoft or Oracle or the NeoClouds, which implemented full NVIDIA solutions, instead of the standalone HGX RECs that AWS favored, were much better suited to training large language models. That is still the case, by the way. What has changed is that training is no longer the biggest AI compute market. Inference is, thanks not only to increased AI adoption, but also because of fundamental changes in terms of how AI works. From an update about Nvidia: “the first inflection point was the emergence of LLMs. Call this the ChatGPT moment. In this first paradigm tokens were generated by GPUs and presented as the answer to a question. The second inflection point was the emergence of reasoning models. Call this the O1 moment. In this paradigm, there are a very large number of tokens that are generated to figure out the answer before the answer is actually generated. This was an exponential increase in the addressable market for tokens. The third inflection point was the emergence of functional agents — call this the Opus 4.5 moment. In this paradigm, those reasoning models are not triggered by humans asking a question, but by an agent solving a problem. This increases the market in two directions. First, humans can run multiple agents. And secondly, agents can leverage reasoning models multiple times to accomplish a task. This isn't just an exponential increase in the addressable market for tokens. It's two exponential increases squared.” Both the shift to inference and the shift in the nature of inference have been positives for AWS’s approach. First, while inference still requires significant memory, the requirement is significantly less than that required for training. It's actually viable to store a model’s parameters in a single server. You don't need to network together thousands of chips. Second, while reasoning and agentic workloads require significantly more tokens, and thus a massively larger KV cache, the increase is actually so large that even the most optimized NVIDIA inference systems are being built with dedicated memory servers. This sort of architecture is much more compatible with Amazon’s networking approach than the thousands-of-chips-networked-together approach is. Third, agents are heavily CPU dependent, which has two important implications. First, fully utilizing accelerators is a function of having sufficient general compute. Second, achieving maximum utilization of heterogeneous compute means unbundling CPUs and GPUs and routing workloads between resources, which is exactly the sort of disaggregated resource abstraction that Amazon has been building with Nitro. The utilization point is an important one. NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang made his case for NVIDIA chips over custom ASICs at length at GTC 2025. Huang’s argument was that AI factories, to use his term, were ultimately constrained by power. That meant that the most important metric for profitability was not the cost of chips, but rather tokens per watt. In other words, if you can’t increase watts, it’s worth spending more on chips to increase tokens on those watts. There are, however, three reasons why this argument may not hold, particularly for a company like Amazon. First, if you have the money to buy that many NVIDIA chips, you also have the money to spend on getting more power, which is exactly what AWS has been focused on. This very much fits AWS’s modus operandi, which is to invest more upstream, in this case in power, with the goal of spending less downstream, paying NVIDIA huge margins for their chips. Second, in the long term, electricity is more of a commodity than logic is. That means it is a market where innovation and competition are more likely to break a bottleneck, which is another way to say that investing in one’s own silicon is the area most likely to deliver a return on investment. Third, the nature of inference workloads — particularly agentic ones — are such that perfect accelerator utilization is going to be a much harder problem to solve than when it comes to training. These points are moot, however, if you don't have your own logic chip that is at least competitive, and here Amazon’s long-term outlook is paying off. Amazon bought Annapurna Labs, which makes their chips, in 2015, and launched their first AI-focused chip in 2019. No, it wasn't very good, but critically, that was seven years ago. Now Trainium 3 is decent and the trajectory is even better. AWS is poised to have a sustainable cost advantage for inference going forward. AWS’s Neutrality. Moreover, they are already replaying the Graviton playbook: Trainium chips help undergird Bedrock, their AI platform, which is to say that users are using Trainium chips even if they didn't explicitly choose to do so. AWS CEO Matt Garman made this point explicitly in a Stratechery interview: “I think just with GPUs, by the way, you're going to interact with a lot of these accelerator chips through abstractions. So the vast majority of customers don't interact with GPUs either, except through maybe like in their laptop or something like that, but you know, for graphics. But when you're talking to OpenAI, even if they're running on GPUs, you're not talking to the GPUs; if you're talking to Claude, you're through GPUs or Trainium or TPUs, you're not talking to any of those chips, you're talking to the interface. And the vast majority of inference out there is being done on one of a handful of models, right? And so whether it's 5, 10, 20, 100, it's not millions of people that are programming to those things directly, and that's going to be true going forward just because these systems are so complex, they're very large. If you're going to go train a model, not that many people have enough money to go train a model, not that many people have the expertise to actually manage it. They're very complicated systems. And the OpenAI team is incredible in their ability to squeeze value out of a very large compute cluster. But not that many people have the team that can do that, independent of what the chip happens to be. And so I think that that's going to be true for all accelerator chips, honestly.” The frontier models are an important factor in this, and this is an angle I didn't see coming. NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang explained in a recent interview with Dwarkesh Patel why NVIDIA didn't invest in Anthropic early on: “At the time, I didn't deeply internalize how difficult it would be to build a foundation AI lab like OpenAI and Anthropic, and the fact that they needed huge investments from the supplier themselves. We just weren't in a position to make the multi-billion dollar investment into Anthropic so that they could use our compute. But Google and AWS were, and they put in huge investments in the beginning so that Anthropic in return used their compute. We just weren't in a position to do so at the time, nor did I—I would say my mistake is I didn't deeply internalize that they really had no other options, that a VC would never put in $5-10 billion of investment into an AI lab with the hopes of it turning out to be Anthropic. And so that was my miss. But even if I understood it, I don't think we would have been in a position to do that at the time. But I’m not going to make that same mistake again.” Amazon had both the money and the chips to invest into Anthropic precisely because they had built such a cash machine with AWS in the first place. That's the thing with big investments in infrastructure: they take years to build, but the benefit of that investment compounds over time. Anthropic, meanwhile, thanks to those investments from Amazon and Google, can not only run across a variety of chips, but for a long time was the only frontier model available on all of the leading clouds. An important selling point for enterprises. Microsoft in the end needed to let go of Azure’s exclusive access to OpenAI’s API, in part because that exclusivity was hurting the prospects of their mammoth stake in OpenAI. You can also make the case that Amazon is the best choice for frontier model access in a world of limited compute. Microsoft’s core business is software, which is to say that the company faces massive pressure to invest in their own AI capabilities, even at the cost of deprioritizing cloud customers. That’s exactly what happened at Microsoft earlier this year, when the company missed Azure’s growth projections because they devoted more compute to their internal workloads. It was an understandable decision: cloud demand is eternal, but the risk from AI for existing software businesses is existential. This also applies to Google. The company’s core business is also digital, and while search has fended off the threat from chatbots that many expected, the fundamental challenge is still one to be managed, not extinguished. Amazon’s core businesses, meanwhile, are very much rooted in the physical world: selling and shipping physical goods, and building data centers. Both are amenable to Amazon devoting the majority of its chips to customers’ workloads. Amazon’s Future. If this week marks the resolution of one of Amazon’s long bets, you can see the outline of future resolutions in present-day announcements. One prominent example is Amazon Leo, the company’s satellite service that seems, at first glance, duplicative to SpaceX’s Starlink, which has the advantage of already existing at scale. Remember Amazon’s formula, however, which CEO Andy Jassy stated explicitly with regards to Leo on the company’s most recent earnings call: “Today, if you ask what stops us from growing the business, we have to get the constellation into space. We have over 20 launches planned this year, we have over 30 launches planned in 2027. But I think the business has a chance to be a very large, many-billion-dollar revenue business. And I think it has some characteristics that are reminiscent of AWS in that it’s capital-intensive up front, where you’re committing a lot of capital and cash in the early years for assets that you get to leverage over a long period of time. So I like the free cash flow and return on invested capital characteristics of that business in the medium to long term.” The fact it is extremely capital-intensive is not the only thing about Leo that makes it like AWS. A critical factor is that Amazon is the first best customer to give this service scale. And here it’s worth going back to logistics. I noted above that Amazon delivery still has marginal costs, and that is because humans have to make the delivery. Amazon, however, has already pointed to the future a full 13 years ago when the company first started talking publicly about drone delivery. It’s been a long slog, to be sure, but it’s increasingly plausible to imagine a future where delivery costs are a matter of depreciation on drone assets. And what would such a future require? How about reliable widespread satellite coverage for communicating with and guiding those drones? And if Amazon doesn't want to be dependent on Jensen Huang for chips, do you think they want to be dependent on Elon Musk for drone connectivity? Of course other businesses, like Apple, will be able to pay to use Amazon’s satellite infrastructure, just like they can now pay to use Amazon’s delivery service, or pay to use AWS, or pay to sell on Amazon.com. The world may change in increasingly drastic ways, but Amazon’s approach, by virtue of its focus on long-term investments in the physical world, appears to be as sturdy as ever. More generally, I increasingly suspect that long-term vulnerability to AI, or to put it more positively, long-term incentives to invest in AI, are very strongly correlated with the degree to which a company interacts with the physical world, and secondarily, the degree to which companies feel secure in their control of distribution. Apple and Amazon feel comfortable not having leading-edge models, just access to them, because their business is rooted in the physical. Microsoft has invested heavily in data centers, but doesn't own their own model, perhaps because they feel their control of distribution to enterprises will protect their core business, or because they had too much of a dependency on OpenAI. Google and Meta are investing at a similar scale to Amazon, and also heavily invested in their own models. Both are Aggregators, which is to say they have to continually earn attention from consumers, given that competition is only a click away; having good AI is existential to them. This is, in the end, another advantage to making the sort of long-term bets Amazon specializes in: the threats are so distant that you have plenty of time to make new investments that address any weaknesses that develop in the meantime, or as is the case of AI, wait for the market to tilt in your favor.

Anduril CEO Weighs on $61 Billion Round

Bloomberg Technology(724.0K)·11:36·9.0K views·2026-05-13
Brian SchimpfCEO, Anduril Ed LudlowHost, Bloomberg Technology Caroline HydeHost, Bloomberg Technology
TL;DR"Production is the name of the game.
Anduril has raised $5 billion to aggressively scale "Arsenal One" and its manufacturing capacity, focusing on high-volume, low-cost munitions like the new Barracuda cruise missile to address critical global shortages and the "2027 window of danger" in the Indo-Pacific. ...

TL;DR: Anduril has raised $5 billion to aggressively scale "Arsenal One" and its manufacturing capacity, focusing on high-volume, low-cost munitions like the new Barracuda cruise missile to address critical global shortages and the "2027 window of danger" in the Indo-Pacific.

Production Scaling and "Arsenal One"

  • Capital Allocation: The $5 billion raise is primarily dedicated to scaling factories, expanding the production workforce, and funding inventory to meet a massive ramp in demand.
  • Revenue Growth: Brian Schimpf reports that production revenue is increasing approximately 250% year-over-year.
  • Manufacturing Philosophy:
      • Arsenal One: A move away from "exquisite," expensive systems toward mass-producible defense tech.
      • Private vs. Public Funding: Schimpf credits the American private capital system for allowing the company to fund "aggressive ramps" faster than traditional government or public market timelines.

Strategic Shift in Munitions & Defense

  • Barracuda Cruise Missile: Anduril recently announced a multi-year procurement deal for thousands of these low-cost cruise missiles annually to solve the critical munitions shortage facing the U.S. and its allies.
  • High-Demand Sectors: The company is seeing massive demand for counter-drone technology, air and missile defense, and electronic warfare systems, driven largely by lessons from conflicts in Ukraine and Iran.
  • Addressing Consumption Rates:
      • Inventory Crisis: Schimpf noted that the U.S. recently consumed roughly a decade's worth of Tomahawk production in just 72 hours.
      • Solution: Using commercial technologies to build smarter, cheaper systems (e.g., surface-to-air missiles) that can be replenished at a national scale.

IPO Outlook and Secondary Market Risks

  • IPO Rationale: Schimpf stated there is "no rush" to go public because private capital markets are now large enough to fund high-growth businesses at scale.
      • Liquidity: The company can provide liquidity to employees and early investors through straightforward private means without the pressure of an IPO.
      • Success Metrics: Anduril intends to go public only after it has fully proven its high-margin, high-growth economics at scale.
  • Secondary Market Warning:
      • Fraudulent Activity: Schimpf echoed concerns regarding "bad actors" and brokers misrepresenting access to private shares, describing it as an issue affecting many high-profile private tech companies.
      • Cap Table Control: The company maintains tight control over its cap table to ensure investors are aligned for the "long haul."

Geopolitical Deterrence and Taiwan

  • The "2027 Window": Schimpf acknowledges 2027 as a "period of maximum danger" where the PRC (China) will have established significant military capability relative to a disadvantaged U.S. and Taiwan.
  • Deterrence Strategy:
      • Anduril is actively providing technology to Taiwan to bolster deterrence.
      • The goal is to provide the military with enough volume and "intelligent targeting" capabilities to preserve the status quo and prevent conflict through strength.

eToro Group Ltd Q1 2026 Earnings Call

Record Earnings: Net contribution reached...

TL;DR

eToro delivered record Q1 2026 results with a 35% year-over-year increase in adjusted EBITDA, driven by a successful strategic pivot from crypto to commodities and the aggressive integration of AI-powered "agentic" trading tools.

Financial Performance and Key Metrics

  • Record Earnings: Net contribution reached $258 million (up 19% YoY), while adjusted EBITDA grew to $109 million, representing a 42% margin.
  • User Growth: Funded accounts increased 12% YoY to 4.02 million, marking the fastest organic growth in over a year. Momentum continued into April with accounts reaching 4.07 million.
  • Assets Under Administration (AUA): AUA grew 15% YoY to $17 billion by the end of Q1, accelerating further to $18.7 billion in April.
  • Capital Allocation: The company maintains a strong balance sheet with $1.3 billion in cash and equivalents. In Q1, eToro repurchased 3.3 million shares for approximately $103 million.

Multi-Asset Strategy and Market Resilience

  • Commodities Surge: Reflecting a shift in market volatility, commodities accounted for 60% of trading commissions in Q1. Trading volumes in this sector increased nearly fourfold year-over-year.
  • Diversification Advantage: The "multi-asset model" successfully retained users during the crypto downturn; 40% of customers who traded commodities in Q1 moved back into equities or crypto in April.
  • Expanded Access: eToro rolled out 24/7 trading for select commodities, stocks, and indices, noting that roughly 30% of stock trading volume has shifted to after-market hours.
  • Regional Highlights: The company expanded its UK presence with a record quarter for Cash ISAs (AUA grew 15x YoY) and successfully launched Japanese equities.

AI Innovation and the "Agentic" Roadmap

  • Internal Transformation: CEO Yoni Assia declared 2026 the "year of agents," noting that AI agents are now integrated into every internal function (engineering, marketing, research), significantly accelerating product development cycles.
  • Tori and Agent Portfolios: eToro launched "Tori," an AI investing agent that allows users to define parameters while the AI manages the portfolio.
  • App Store and Ecosystem: The new eToro App Store and Builders Portal allow developers to distribute AI-driven trading tools and "agentic" strategies to the platform's millions of users.
  • SpaceX AI Partnership: The company expanded its partnership with SpaceX AI to embed real-time market sentiment powered by the Grok 4.2 model into Tori's investment workflows.

Strategic Acquisitions and US Outlook

  • Crypto Strategy: Despite the current market cycle, eToro acquired Zengo, a self-custodial wallet provider, to bridge traditional investing with DeFi and on-chain assets.
  • US Market Progress: eToro activated its BitLicense and money transmitter license in New York. The company expects a full rollout of its CopyTrader and Smart Portfolios in the US in H2 2026, with commodities trading expected to follow in 6–9 months.
  • M&A Pipeline: Management signaled a "very strong" M&A pipeline for the remainder of 2026, viewing crypto downturns as opportunistic windows for accretive acquisitions.

Notable Quotes

"The internet democratized information, blockchain democratized value, and AI is now democratizing financial expertise. AI will help millions of people participate in the markets with greater confidence and better tools." — Yoni Assia, CEO
"Our diversified business model has demonstrated resilience across market cycles... 40% of the customers who traded commodities in Q1 also traded stocks or crypto in April." — Meron Shani, CFO
"Six months ago, we made AI a company-wide mandate. We didn’t just adopt tools, we rethought how we operate. Work that historically took quarters now ships in a fraction of the time." — Yoni Assia, CEO

The impact is significant. We're already seeing significant productivity gains in engineering with more to come, and similar acceleration is extending across the rest of the organization. Work that historically took quarters now ships in a fraction of the time. Capabilities that used to require deep technical expertise are becoming accessible across the business. Over the past six months, users who initially traded crypto or equities accounted for most of the commodities trading volume. That is the multi-asset model in action and it is how we deepen user engagement over time. The strength and resilience of eToro's business model are anchored in our proven ability to sell across a diversified multi-asset platform, a pattern we've successfully executed through both crypto rallies and periods of elevated activity in traditional capital markets. Our 24/5 trading offering launched last year and is being widely used, and it underscores the global nature of our user base. This year we took the next step. We see this as an evolution in capital markets. Traditional markets are starting to look like crypto markets, and users want to trade on their own schedule. Turning to investing, copy trading remains a core differentiator for eToro as we're the only company offering automated agentic trading. This quarter copy trading reached an all-time high, driven by rising demand to copy and pro investors who are actively reacting to volatile market events. Copy trading is widely used by our users, reinforcing its role as a cornerstone of our social investing model. As an AI-first company, AI-powered investing sits at the core center of our innovation strategy. We're entering a new era of investing where AI is reshaping how individuals access markets, make decisions and build wealth. At eToro, we see AI as a force that levels the playing field, giving every investor access to capabilities once reserved only for institutions. Our vision is to equip each user with their own team of AI agents—tools that analyze the markets, generate insights and share personalized analysis, while enabling them to build, share and scale strategies within a global collaborative investing ecosystem. We're extending the same agent architecture we have built internally directly to our users by putting institution-grade capabilities into the hands of every retail investor on eToro, designed to seamlessly integrate with the community they already trust to learn from and engage with. Ultimately, AI is accelerating a long-term trend of making investing more accessible, more social and more personalized. AI will help millions of people participate in the markets with greater confidence, better tools and a stronger connection to both data and community. The introduction of agent portfolios is also a meaningful step on that journey. Agent portfolios let users allocate capital to AI-driven strategies inside a dedicated environment in their eToro account. Through Tori, our AI investing agent and conversational interface, users define the parameters and the AI agents run the portfolio. They get exposure to intelligent portfolio management in a controlled and transparent way while keeping full control over their broader investments. As part of our AI efforts, we also recently expanded our partnership with SpaceX AI by embedding real-time X intelligence and market sentiment powered by the frontier Grok 4.2 model directly into the investment workflows of Tori. As more activity shifts toward automated AI-driven strategies, we expect increased engagement, trading activity and volume across the platform. Building on this, we have launched the eToro App Store, a marketplace for trading and analytics applications directly within the eToro ecosystem. Net trading contribution from crypto was $13 million with a year-over-year decline driven primarily by lower trading activity and customers shifting to trade commodities. As we have seen in prior crypto cycles, these periods of volatility are expected and our diversified business model has demonstrated resilience across market cycles. Our crypto net contribution includes a $5 million negative valuation impact of our corporate crypto holdings, resulting in a balance of $14 million at the end of the quarter. Net interest income contributed $48 million, down 5% year-over-year, largely driven by a lower interest rate environment and user deleveraging amid market volatility. The decline was partially offset by a 13% increase in higher interest-earning assets as a result of an increase in user cash deposits, staking and corporate cash. eToro Money's contribution grew 32% year-over-year to a record $29 million, driven by a 70% year-over-year increase in total money transfers as we continue to experience increased deposits and user activity. In the first quarter, adjusted OPEX was $150 million, up 7% quarter-on-quarter, driven by $12 million increase in customer acquisition costs. Our adjusted selling and marketing expenses for the quarter was $58 million or 22% of net contribution. As discussed last quarter, given the strength of our cohort returns and our objective to accelerate growth in 2026, we plan to increase our sales and marketing investment from 21% last year, scaling gradually to 25% of net contribution this year. Adjusted R&D, G&A and operating expenses for the quarter were $38 million and $54 million respectively. Our adjusted diluted EPS for the quarter was 91 cents compared to 77 cents in the first quarter of 2025. Moving to our balance sheet, we ended the quarter with $1.3 billion in cash, cash equivalents and short-term investments and generated $104 million of cash from operating activities during the quarter. 'He Is Uniquely Unqualified To Lead': Elizabeth Warren Drops The Hammer On Fed Chair Nom Kevin Warsh

Forbes Breaking News(5.6M)·10:03·1.7K views·2026-05-13
Elizabeth WarrenUS Senator (D-MA) Kevin WarshFed Chair Nominee
TL;DRSenator Elizabeth Warren is urging the Senate to reject Kevin Warsh’s nomination for Federal Reserve Chair, arguing he is a "sock puppet" for President Trump who lacks the independence, transparency, and historical judgment necessary to lead the nation’s central bank.

TL;DR

Senator Elizabeth Warren is urging the Senate to reject Kevin Warsh’s nomination for Federal Reserve Chair, arguing he is a "sock puppet" for President Trump who lacks the independence, transparency, and historical judgment necessary to lead the nation’s central bank.

Political Interference and Independence Concerns

  • Systemic Pressure: Warren characterizes Warsh’s nomination as the culmination of a "ridiculous scheme" by the Trump administration to "juice" the economy for political gain via "bogus" criminal investigations into sitting Fed officials like Chair Jay Powell and Governor Lisa Cook.
  • Lack of Neutrality: During his confirmation hearing, Warsh reportedly refused to acknowledge that Donald Trump lost the 2020 election, leading Warren to argue he operates in a "delusional alternative reality" rather than one based on economic data.
  • Unprecedented Opposition: Warren noted that every Democrat on the Banking Committee voted against Warsh, marking the first party-line vote for a Fed Chair nominee in history.

Financial Transparency and Conflicts of Interest

  • Secret Assets: Warsh has refused to disclose the sources of over $100 million in assets, which would make him the wealthiest Fed Chair in history while remaining "entangled" with unknown entities.
  • Potential Ethics Violations: Warren raised concerns that Warsh may currently own bank stocks, which would be a direct violation of the Federal Reserve Act upon his swearing-in.
  • Billionaire Ties: There are allegations that billionaire Stanley Druckenmiller—who profits from betting on Fed movements—may be involved in "cashing out" Warsh’s secret assets, creating a potential "sweetheart deal" just as Warsh takes office.
  • Specific Entanglements: Warsh refused to answer if he holds investments in Chinese-controlled companies, Jeffrey Epstein-affiliated vehicles, or businesses tied to the Trump family.

Historical Economic Failures (2008 Crisis)

  • Negligent Oversight: As a Fed Governor during the lead-up to the 2008 crash, Warsh ignored explicit warnings regarding subprime mortgages and foreclosures, stating as late as 2007 that he saw no "immediate systemic risk" among big banks.
  • Pro-Wall Street Bias: Warren criticized Warsh’s role as "Wall Street’s personal liaison," claiming he prioritized multi-billion dollar bank bailouts over the 8 million workers who lost jobs and 10 million families who lost homes.
  • Deficient Judgment: Warsh championed risky financial instruments like credit default swaps, calling them "innovations" that made the system safer shortly before they contributed to the global economic collapse.

Policy Reversals and "Quid Pro Quo"

  • Interest Rate Volatility: For over a decade, Warsh was an "inflation hawk," pushing for high interest rates even as the economy struggled to recover.
  • Sudden Pivot: Warren alleges Warsh abandoned these long-held principles as soon as Trump re-entered office, "shouting from the rooftops" for interest rate cuts in exchange for the nomination.
"At this unprecedented moment in the Fed's history, he [Kevin Warsh] is uniquely unqualified to lead the agency." — Sen. Elizabeth Warren
"Subprime mortgages have gotten a bad name in this environment and in some cases that is not just." — Kevin Warsh (quoted by Sen. Warren from 2007)
"The American people would be right to question who Mr. Warsh will serve if a billionaire gives him a sweetheart deal just as Mr. Warsh takes office." — Sen. Elizabeth Warren

Third, Warsh says that he has, quote, "no regrets" from his catastrophic tenure as a Fed governor before, during, and after the 2008 financial crash. The Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission found that Governor Warsh attended multiple meetings in 2006 in which participants rung the alarm about subprime mortgages and rising foreclosures. Participants implored the Fed to use its authority to address these risks. Not only did Mr. Warsh ignore these warnings, he pushed back against them. In fact, in 2007, he said, quote, "Subprime mortgages have gotten a bad name in this environment and in some cases that is not just." Mr. Warsh's number one priority was the big banks that crashed our economy. He worked tirelessly to orchestrate multi-billion dollar Wall Street bailouts. But Mr. Warsh says today, he has no regrets. After the crash, most people on the Fed saw the economic devastation and said, "Now might be a good time to lower interest rates to revive the economy." Not Mr. Warsh. Nope. He wanted to keep interest rates high. No regrets. And he kept pushing for higher interest rates for more than a decade, even as our economy struggled. When the job of Fed chair became available during Trump's first term as president, Donald Trump passed him by. Now, Mr. Warsh chalked this up to his refusal to, quote, "put his ambitions ahead of his principles." Well, Mr. Warsh decided not to make that mistake the second time around. As soon as Donald Trump became president a second time, Mr. Warsh began shouting from the rooftops that the Fed should cut interest rates. In exchange for abandoning his principles, the President offered Mr. Warsh his dream job. Look, red lights are flashing all across our economy. American families are struggling. Republicans will come to regret their decision to aid and abet President Trump's Fed takeover by installing Mr. Warsh as chair. I urge a no vote. Thank you, Mr. President. I yield the floor.

How Claude went from $9 billion to $30 billion in one year | CFO explains

Invest Like The Best(63.3K)·82:04·35.7K views·2026-05-13
Patrick O'ShaughnessyHost, Invest Like The Best Krishna RaoCFO, Anthropic
TL;DRAnthropic CFO Krishna Rao details the company’s explosive growth from a $9 billion to $30 billion revenue run rate in a single year, driven by a strategic "compute-first" philosophy that prioritizes staying at the technological frontier to unlock massive enterprise demand.

TL;DR

Anthropic CFO Krishna Rao details the company’s explosive growth from a $9 billion to $30 billion revenue run rate in a single year, driven by a strategic "compute-first" philosophy that prioritizes staying at the technological frontier to unlock massive enterprise demand.

The Compute Strategy: "Lifeblood of the Business"

  • Compute as a Canvas: Krishna Rao spends 30–40% of his time on compute procurement and allocation, describing it as the foundational canvas upon which all AI products are built.
  • Chip Fungibility and Flexibility: Anthropic has invested years in building an orchestration layer that allows them to use NVIDIA GPUs, Google TPUs, and Amazon Trainium chips interchangeably.
      • Collaborative Hardware Development: The company works directly with teams like Amazon’s Annapurna Labs to influence chip roadmaps, ensuring their "dollar of compute" goes further than competitors.
  • The Allocation Hierarchy: Management meets constantly to balance compute across three internal buckets:
      • Model Training: This has a non-negotiable "floor"; the company will never sacrifice frontier research for short-term revenue.
      • Internal Acceleration: High priority is given to internal use; 90%+ of Anthropic's code is now written by Claude.
      • Customer Inference: Serving the rapidly growing external demand.

Managing the "Cone of Uncertainty" and Exponential Growth

  • Non-Linear Forecasting: Rao highlights the difficulty of financial planning when weekly growth rates compound into wildly different outcomes.
      • Revenue Milestone: Anthropic grew from a $9 billion run rate to over $30 billion in run rate revenue in just the first quarter of 2024.
  • The Jevons Paradox in AI: Anthropic found that lowering the price of the "Opus" model class did not lead to lower revenue; instead, it triggered a massive surge in consumption as customers found the price-performance sweet spot for complex workloads.
  • Frontier Intelligence Thesis: Rao argues that in the enterprise sector, the "returns to frontier intelligence" are extremely high. Every leap in model IQ unlocks a new Total Addressable Market (TAM) by enabling long-horizon tasks and agentic capabilities that previous versions could not handle.

Internal Transformation and "The Virtual Collaborator"

  • The Finance "Insight Engine": The Anthropic finance team uses Claude to automate the production of statutory financial statements and monthly financial reviews (MFRs).
      • Skill Library: The team has a repository of 70+ specific "skills" for Claude, reducing tasks that once took hours down to 30 minutes.
  • High Engagement: The most senior members of the finance team, including the Head of Tax, are often the highest "token consumers," using AI to automate complex policy and accounting workloads.
  • The Future of Work: Rao envisions the "Virtual Collaborator"—AI agents with memory and context that don't just complete tasks but manage entire ideas over long time horizons.

Culture, Safety, and Competitive Advantage

  • Safety as a Commercial Asset: While Anthropic invests heavily in "Interpretability" (the MRI for models) and "Alignment" for safety reasons, these features have become major selling points for the Fortune 10. Enterprises trust Claude because the company can explain how and why the model makes decisions.
  • Talent Density over Mass: All seven co-founders remain at the company. Anthropic prioritizes "intellectual honesty" and a "culture of writing," where deep research is debated transparently across the organization.
  • Capital Formation: Anthropic has raised $7.5 billion since Rao joined, with another $5 billion committed via Google and Amazon. The massive capital raises are described as a hedge against the "cone of uncertainty" rather than a requirement to fund current operational losses.

Notable Quotes

"The compute that we procure is the lifeblood of our business. It is the most important thing in the company. It is the thing on which everything else gets built... if you buy too much compute, you go out of business. If you buy too little compute, you can't serve your customers and you're not at the frontier." — Krishna Rao
"We don't really think about models as like closed or open; we think of them as frontier or not. And the ones that are at the frontier clearly are capturing this economic value and driving meaningful ROI for customers." — Krishna Rao
"Humans mostly think linearly and you think incrementally. That's a paradigm I've had to break for myself... When you're on this exponential, the range of outcomes starts to be really, really wide." — Krishna Rao
"Right now, within the company, 90+ percent of our code is actually written by Claude... the models themselves are helping us to build that next generation of models." — Krishna Rao
Every time we have a new model, there's a set of capabilities that are different. People tend to think about model intelligence as IQ. We think of it kind of differently. Intelligence for us is multidimensional. It's not just a score. What is the real-world capability of this model? Each model generation gives you the chance to do more with it, to do it better, to do it more efficiently, because we think the returns to frontier intelligence are extremely high. And it's extremely high, especially in enterprise. That's a core thesis of our business. It's a key part of what you do, it's a key part of what these companies are doing, and there's just this whole revolution happening. It is the most important thing in the company. It is the canvas on which everything else gets built. And so the decisions we make in how much compute to buy are some of the most consequential and hardest decisions to make in the entire company. So, we talk a lot about this cone of uncertainty, but the idea of just these purchases that have these real-world implications, right? You can't just go out and buy a gigawatt of compute and have it delivered next week. You have to really think ahead to plan for this. And so we really take a very disciplined approach to how we think about it. We look bottoms up. We model what we think demand will be, obviously we sometimes get that wrong. We think about the compute we need to stay at the frontier, and we really look ahead and try to estimate that.
