Number one, we use three different chip platforms. So we are customers of Amazon's Trainium chip, Google's TPUs, and NVIDIA's GPUs. We use these chips fungibly. If you think about the compute we buy, we're using it for model development, we're using it internally to speed up our own product and model development, and then we're also using it obviously to serve customers. Across those three chip platforms, we're using compute for all of those internal and external uses. And that flexibility, it actually took us a long time to be able to do that. We've invested in that over multiple years to be what I believe the most efficient users of compute amongst any of the frontier labs. And that's not something that just happened overnight. When we started using TPUs, I think it was maybe the third generation of TPUs was the first one we used at scale, people thought, "Oh, well, you're crazy, everyone's using GPUs, why aren't you using GPUs?" And we've invested very heavily to be able to use that compute incredibly flexibly. And then we look across the different generations of those chip platforms and use each generation for the best workload internally. And so we've really built this orchestration layer that gives us that flexibility to use all different types of compute, and in doing so, we're also able to get the most value out of it. Am I thinking about this in the right way that something like CUDA that has been a part of NVIDIA's story for a long time now that allows you to do a lot with the underlying actual hardware, that you want to sort of eke your way into being as close to the bare metal as possible and that's part of this flexibility and being able to control as many of the variables as you can? Is that the journey that you've been on? That's part of the journey for sure, but it's also been actually pretty collaborative. So we work really closely with the Annapurna Labs team at Amazon to help to influence the roadmap of these chips, because we believe what we're doing is really stressing the limits of what these chips are capable of. That means that a dollar of compute inside our organization goes further than I think it does anywhere else. But importantly, we basically want to utilize each chip to its best purpose within the company. And so that does mean that we're building our own compilers. We're really building things from the chip level up in order to have that customization and that flexibility to use it internally the way we think will generate the most ROI. And so as we're thinking ahead, even with our revenue growth, it's really hard to predict this business, right? And it's really hard, I think humans mostly think linearly and you think incrementally. How are scaling laws talked about internally? The sort of consensus has been you've got different components of them, you've got like pre-training, you've got post-training, you've got reasoning, and that all of these are kind of moving at different paces and to hit a true wall, they would all need to fall down. That's sort of like how the world is now conceptualizing scaling laws. How are they talked about internally, how do you think about them? Yeah, I mean we look at models at various points in their development. We can see during a pre-training run, how does this model compare to a prior model that we did on these kind of loss curves. And that gives us a sense for model capability. You can do the same thing as you think about RL, and then probably as importantly is when customers get their hands on it. What are they seeing, where are they identifying pain points, and those pain points then become like training targets for us. We don't train on customer data on the enterprise side. On the prosumer side, it's only if you opt in. Where do you think the investors as a group kind of like understand it now, and where are there misunderstandings about Anthropic? Tell us that side of your life. So I joined the company about two years ago. We were closing our Series D at the time. You know, look, that was not a straightforward fundraising. The company really only had a frontier model in the middle of that fundraising. Towards the tail end of it, the FTX transaction was happening, which liquidated a bunch of Anthropic shares. They were also around, you know, our mission and how we approach things. People said, "Well, hey, aren't AI safety and building a really big business, aren't those things at odds?" There were also a lot of other misconceptions on, "Your sales force is really small, don't you need to scale it like all these enterprise software companies?" And so there was just a bit of a paradigm around trying to fit us, you know, into a particular mold that had existed before. Over time, you know, it's evolved. We at the end of 2024 raised the Series E. You know, the business had scaled to close to a billion dollars of run rate revenue. But the day of our first close was the day the DeepSeek news came out. Obviously we got the close done, but certainly a ton of volatility as people then said, "Wait a minute, should I just totally re-underwrite how I think about AI in total?" And so that was the Series E. Obviously we brought on, you know, great investors across all of these. But people still had some of those questions. They looked at our forecast and they thought, "Okay, you know, I get it, you've grown to a billion dollars of run rate revenue so quickly, but there's no way you're going to be able to keep that up." Yeah, it's just not possible, right? And there's laws of physics and you're in enterprise which is great, but the adoption's going to take so much longer. I mean, look at how long it took with Cloud and how many people are still on-prem. The business continued to prove out the thesis that the return to frontier intelligence is really high. We are really focused on what's really happened, which is model-led growth enabled by products and our go-to-market team and our distribution. And then I think what they also saw was that this thesis of, "Hey, it's really important to build this transformative technology but to do it in the right way and do it responsibly," had this really interesting interlink with our business that most people didn't really understand or really believe. That was that we invest in research not just in model development but also in AI safety research, right? Like we've pioneered interpretability, which is—think of it as like an MRI for the model to see inside the neural network how it works. We pioneered alignment science, which is: you want the model to do what you tell it to, and how often does it do that and how often does it stray from that? And those things are important for our mission and that's why we did them, but they had these downstream effects where it turns out if you can look inside the model, you're better at building them. And then the last linkage: if you're selling to enterprises, like we now sell to nine of the Fortune 10. All of those enterprises are entrusting us with, you know, customer information, with their data. They're interacting with their employees, sometimes even interacting with their customers as well. Those are the most sensitive workloads. More and more of these businesses are running on Claude and our Claude platform. When you have this investment that we've made and will continue to make in safety, interpretability, alignment, like that actually inures to the benefit of the enterprise customers as well. Because if they're going to entrust us with all that access and all that data and the ability to kind of work in the most sensitive workloads within their company, they want a company that they can trust. And that's not why we invested in it, but it did have this kind of downstream effect that we've really seen prove out again and again: to be a company that is both at the frontier but one that is investing in safety and that you can trust. We've raised, you know, $7.5 billion since I joined the company. We have another $5 billion that'll come in in the future from the Amazon and Google deals that we inked last month. And so that's a tremendous amount of capital. But it's a capital-intensive business and we need this capital to support that growth. But you know, it all goes to the fact that the business is running very efficiently, and so the reason we raise this capital is more because of that cone of uncertainty than it is to fund actual losses in the business today. How was your own perception of this like TAM perspective for a 10X growth of the business? Like the first time that happened, like did you personally believe that it was possible? Like did that seem absurd? And has it continued? Like now that it's becoming consistent and so maybe it's becoming more commonplace to you or something? But what was your like own view staring at this cone about like the odds of hitting, you know, a 10X type of growth so many years in a row? Well, when I joined the business it had about $250 million of run rate revenue and the plan was to get to a billion and I said, "Great, in what year?" And that was like linear thinking, right? And you know, consistently Dario has been a much better predictor of the revenue than I have. I think we're going to close the gap over time as we get better at forecasting and understanding the business. But yeah, definitely the first time I saw it, you have all these arguments about the laws of physics and law of large numbers and this can't—you know, where is the revenue coming from and how can it be added this quickly and how can customers move this quickly and is this even possible in enterprise? And all of those things start to get broken down over time as you see how the business works internally. You see how the adoption curves and the exponentials that are happening—again, we have the exponential that's happening on revenue, but underlying it are these many other exponentials that support that. You start to see and believe in that more. Now that doesn't mean we're not disciplined and thoughtful about the forecast and how we think about the range of scenarios. But it does mean that my thinking is at least shifted a lot more from linear and incremental towards leaning into this exponential and really, you know, believing in its potential and how this is just different than how other businesses have evolved. As you've talked to investors at every stage, I'm sure every round that you've raised, there's some thing that is like the most common or hardest thing to explain to investors or that they're struggling the most to understand and get their heads around. What is that today? I think it is this paradigm of how compute is used. Thinking of it as, you know, not just something that is a variable cost over some time period, but really this resource that's so fungibly utilized, right? We run workloads on one day in the morning on a chip for inference and in the afternoon or evening we use it for model development. That is something that paradigm does not exist in a company like a software company or a factory, right? If you have a bunch of people doing R&D and that's your R&D expense, they can't go and become COGS, right? And vice versa in most traditional companies. Here, you really have that fungibility that's possible and I think that's where the return on compute is so important. I think people are beginning to understand that, but there's still a tendency towards treating it like, "Oh, I have to separate these two costs," when in actuality, they're very self-reinforcing and that flexibility is actually what helps to drive revenue short term and long term. If I was to force you out of your role and into an investor seat at a great big investing firm and then I said, "Your job is to go grill these companies and invest in the best ones," like what questions would you be asking of the labs or companies that are building models to really get at the heart of the points of uncertainty, of skepticism, of things that might not make these the best businesses of all time? I'm curious maybe from that angle how you would approach it. So I would say a couple of things. So first, what is the ROI on compute kind of all-up? Like on the way here I was in an Uber and I signed two double-digit million dollar commits like in the car ride, which was like 20 minutes. There was this crazy stat about AI, just the generic concept being less popular than like Congress amongst like the general population. It doesn't seem like the general world that isn't in technology—doesn't live in the Bay Area or New York—does not yet feel or understand why this is good for them just as measured by their opinion of it. What do you think we need to do as an industry about that problem? Look, I think that if we think about the transformation that's happening, there's been other transformative waves before. All the way back to the industrial revolution, the internet, cloud, etc. I think what's one of the things that's different about AI is it's all happening so quickly. You can have years or decades of progress that are being compressed into months. And going back to humans thinking in terms of exponentials versus linear, that can be jarring. If I feel like somebody's just telling me all the good news and none of the bad news then I'm like, "Okay, do I really trust this perspective?" I think that's where there's a need for balance and to say, "Look, these are some of the things that happen when change is compressed over a short amount of time. How do we work across commercial and government to actually come up with some of the solutions?" So I think it's about a clear articulation of the opportunities. It's about really thinking about what those solutions may be, and that's not any one company that can come up with it. We don't have the blueprint that's going to solve everything. But to at least have that dialogue about some of the risks and downsides and what we can do to address it. And then I think it's being transparent about both of those things when we talk about it. And so over the long term, the opportunity is going to be significantly higher and greater than some of the risks and the downsides that will happen, but that doesn't mean it's going to be perfectly smooth on the curve. To patch code bases. You've seen these examples where we had an open-source code base that a prior model found twenty-two security vulnerabilities in and Mythos then found two hundred and fifty. As you're having conversations with people internally, what does the frontier feel like to you? I don't just mean the model frontier. I think it's this idea—and again it's because we're focused on enterprise and because we're really trying to change the productivity of knowledge work that's done in the economy—I think it is towards this vision or this goal of like a virtual collaborator. And so think of this as something that has context within your organization, that can use all of the tools that are specific to you, whether they be homegrown tools or tools that you've purchased. Crazy to imagine. I'm curious how you've had to personally evolve to be able to stay doing this. Introducing Notion’s Developer Platform: Keynote (Ivan Zhao)

Notion(344.0K)·33:38·13.3K views·2026-05-13
Ivan ZhaoFounder, Notion
TL;DRNotion has transitioned from a productivity app to a developer platform, introducing "Workers" to let developers sync any external data source, build deterministic custom tools, and orchestrate multiple external AI agents (like Claude and GitHub) within a single unified workspace.

TL;DR

Notion has transitioned from a productivity app to a developer platform, introducing "Workers" to let developers sync any external data source, build deterministic custom tools, and orchestrate multiple external AI agents (like Claude and GitHub) within a single unified workspace.

The Foundation: Notion Workers and the "ntn" CLI

Notion is moving beyond basic APIs by providing managed infrastructure for developer-written code.

  • Notion Workers: Small, deterministic TypeScript programs deployed into Notion-managed sandboxes. Notion handles deployment, security, and execution, allowing developers to run agent-written software safely.
  • The "ntn" CLI: A new command-line interface designed for both humans and coding agents to authenticate, test, and deploy Workers.
  • Managed Infrastructure: The platform abstracts away complex backend tasks such as OAuth handshakes, environment variable management, and "Pacers" to handle API rate limits.

Data Integration: Syncing External Silos

A new sync capability allows developers to turn Notion into a "shared canvas" by importing data from any system into editable databases.

  • Custom Data Syncs: Developers can now sync data from internal databases (Postgres, Snowflake) or complex enterprise apps (ServiceNow, NetSuite, Stripe) directly into Notion.
  • Observability: A new Terminal User Interface (TUI) provides detailed logs and health metrics for every execution, ensuring business-critical syncs are performing correctly.
  • Collaborative Context: By bringing Stripe billing or Salesforce records into Notion, custom agents can automatically generate reports or flag accounts for human outreach.

Custom Tools and Deterministic Workflows

Beyond fuzzy AI logic, "Tools" allow developers to create reliable, multi-step workflows that save time and cost.

  • Deterministic Execution: Instead of letting an agent "think" through a process (wasting tokens), developers can write "X then Y then Z" workflows in TypeScript and expose them to agents as a single tool.
  • Strict Validation: Workers provide guardrails for internal APIs, ensuring that actions—like toggling a feature flag or accessing a production database—only happen if specific preconditions are met.
  • Native Integration: Tools can be connected to "Custom Agents" through a simple connections menu, allowing non-technical teammates to trigger complex developer workflows.

Agent Orchestration: The Unified UI

Notion aims to solve "UI fragmentation" by allowing external AI agents to live alongside humans in the same documents.

  • External Agents API: A new API surface that allows third-party agents (Claude Code, Codex, Decagon, Amplitude) to appear in the Notion UI as collaborators.
  • Multi-Agent Workflows: A single task can be passed between different specialized agents. For example, a Decagon agent gathers user feedback, a Claude agent writes the code, and a Codex agent performs the PR review.
  • In-House Agents: Companies like Stripe and Ramp can wrap their proprietary internal agents in this API so they can be assigned tasks or tagged in comments just like a teammate.

Notable Quotes

"Notion has always been a place for teams to work and think together. And with AI, that means a team of humans working with a team of agents."

— Ivan Zhao, Co-founder and CEO

"We handle the deployment, execution, security, and governance, all with the assumption that Workers are running agent-written software that you might not have even had time to read."

— Eric, Product Team

"It feels like having infinite minds working day and night to get the busy work done so you and your team can focus on the parts that actually matter."

— Geoffrey, Design Engineer

We've been able to build a system that is easy to use yet sophisticated enough for enterprise-grade syncs by creating a new developer primitive we call Workers. Workers are small TypeScript programs deployed into Notion-managed sandboxes. They're reliable, deterministic, and run on good old CPUs, so you can program Notion in entirely new ways that goes way beyond just syncing. We handle the deployment, execution, security, and governance, all with the assumption that Workers are running agent-written software that you might not have even had time to read. We expose tools and skills so those coding agents can test, introspect, and validate that Workers are doing the exact job you want done. So now, let me show you how to program a Worker to run a sync. Our business team had two requirements before we could connect Workers to software our sales and support teams rely on every day. We needed OAuth and rate limit controls. So we built both into our platform. For OAuth, we've abstracted away the entire handshake and refresh process in our SDK. Just enter a client key and secret as secure environment variables in the OAuth declaration and we will handle the rest. This enables Workers to authenticate with your most sensitive systems on behalf of your team. And our rate limit controls are equally elegant. Just declare a pacer, set your rate limit, and we'll intelligently ensure that all requests stay within the rate limits you define. In fact, the sync skill you saw me use earlier in the Stripe sync helps your coding agent configure a pacer for your use case. And to do that work, both you and agents need shared context you can work in. Syncing, powered by Workers, sets this foundation. Hi everyone, I'm J-Clem, and I'm a software engineer here at Notion. When I first joined Notion, I mostly used it for personal stuff, but I didn't really see it as a developer tool. I loved using it as a reading list and to manage restaurants I wanted to go to. But I didn't find it compelling for work because it didn't integrate well enough with developer systems like GitHub, my preferred logging services, or my actual production databases. At the same time, before the arrival of agents, if you're a developer, many of you may have experienced what I experienced. There was notification after notification where someone was asking me to access some internal system or perform some manual task for them, raise rate limits, enable a feature flag - it was endless. I wanted to be able to automate these tasks in Notion for myself and my teammates, but the problem was that there was this disconnect. Notion didn't work with the tools I needed to perform these tasks. Now, with custom agents powered by Workers and MCP, I can actually begin to unblock my teammates, build tools and workflows for myself, and spend more time focusing on more fun problems. If you're not familiar with Notion custom agents, they combine pages of agent instructions written using Notion's collaborative rich text editor with triggers and actions, many of which are powered by MCPs. Being able to reach for these agents is super powerful, but there are still a few things missing that prevent them from reaching their true potential. Often, an MCP server may lack the tool that I need or I may be using something, and this is especially true of internal APIs, that doesn't have an MCP server available at all. This is why we're introducing another capability of Workers today: Tools. Worker tools are TypeScript functions that allow you to connect your custom agents in Notion to anything you can use via an API, without having to deploy any new services or infrastructure. Third-party APIs, internal APIs, databases, and more. There are a ton of personal use cases too. One Notino uses Workers to control a smart home, and I've seen someone else use them to order groceries. There are other problems that Workers with Tool calls solve as well. They allow you to provide strict validation, whereas MCP servers tend to be relatively unconstrained with their inputs. Since Workers are just TypeScript that you deploy and Notion runs for you, it's easy to put your own guardrails and validations in place. These validations are especially important. Your workflows go from 'well the agent will probably do the right thing' to an absolute guarantee that isn't always possible with MCP servers. Another problem that Tools solves is that often time and money are wasted on running what should otherwise be deterministic workflows using agents. You may have instructions that tell the agent to do X then Y then Z, but why are we wasting tokens letting the agent think between each of these steps? In fact, the more powerful your agent's model is and the higher its thinking effort, the better it's going to do with fuzzy logic, but the more time it's going to waste between those steps that you as a developer know should just happen one after the other. Sometimes, we might try to solve this problem using workflow builder tools. But as workflow complexity grows, the less and less comprehensible these become. We wanted logic and order, but we got a big plate of spaghetti. And often AI agents can feel just shoehorned into these types of flows. All of these problems are what Workers with Agent Tools set out to solve. They allow you or your coding agents to just write these X then Y then Z workflows yourself and then expose them as a single tool to your agent, providing discrete workflows without wasting thinking tokens in between and without having to reach for awkward visual interfaces. Instead, you just get a familiar Turing-complete programming language. Meanwhile, your agent's still there to focus on the things that it's best at: all the fuzzy logic surrounding these workflows. So now that we have an understanding of what Workers' tools are and what they're good at, let's build one. Here's a scenario: Bob, my teammate on customer support, wants to know if custom domains are on for Gummy Bear Aeronautics, his customer. Let's build a worker to solve the problem now that we can share with Bob and his team for future use. First, we need to query our feature flags API to double-check that the flag's actually off for the customer's workspace. We also need a tool that toggles the feature flag, but only after ensuring that the customer is on the default plan for that flag. We'll query our read-only database replica for that. Finally, we want a record of this interaction with the customer in our customer log database in Notion. That way, we contribute to the shared context around this customer, which may be useful to teammates and other agents down the road. To start, I'm going to tell my coding agent, I use Pi these days, what I want to build. Note that a single Worker can have any combination of tools and syncs. It's totally up to you how you want to organize things. Let's look at the Worker that Pi generated. It's a simple example, but let's walk through each part. First we grab our admin API key out of the environment. Remember that we can set these via the Notion CLI and they're stored securely by Notion. Nothing can read them until they're injected just in time into the Worker's environment. Next, we set a title and a description. This shows up for the user in the Notion UI, but it's also used by the agent in tool routing. Then we define our tool's input and provide a description for each argument. This serves as documentation for the custom agent, and it's also used by the Workers platform to validate the input before the tool function's executed. If you've used tool-calling APIs with LLM providers before, this should all look pretty familiar. You'll also notice that the Workers SDK provides a simple object builder syntax. This allows you to easily define your input type without having to remember the complex and verbose syntax of JSON schema. Finally, we have our function that executes when the custom agent calls our tool. We've been careful to leverage TypeScript in useful ways with the Workers SDK, so the function input always matches the exact type of your input definition. In this case, we're just sending a simple HTTP request to our admin API and then returning the result to the agent. Now that we've built our Worker, I'm going to go ahead and use ntn workers deploy to deploy to Notion. As you can see, Notion handles installing dependencies and building the Worker for us. Keep in mind by the way that this is just a simple example. This could be any data source or API from MySQL or Postgres to Snowflake or ClickHouse even. Now we want to enable the custom domains feature flag for Gummy Bear Aeronautics. Here's the outline for the workflow this next tool's going to perform. First, it'll query our feature flag API endpoint for the flag's default plan. In this case, I think custom domains requires an Enterprise plan. Second, we'll query our read-only database replica for the workspace's current plan. We want to ensure that Gummy Bear Aeronautics is on the Enterprise plan. And finally, we'll toggle the flag as long as the custom preconditions are met. We'll skip the code review this time and go right back to our agent, which is connected to our updated Worker. Oh, looks like custom domains is Enterprise only and Gummy Bear Aeronautics is only on the Pro plan. Let's log a report of this to our customer logs database. This is great. Now we've got a durable record of the fact that we tried to enable this for Gummy Bear Aeronautics but that they weren't on the Enterprise plan yet. I'll share this with Bob. This record could also serve as useful context in the future for other teammates and agents. And the best part of this is that I don't have to do this every time anymore. Now that it's built, I can share this custom agent with Bob and the rest of the customer support team and they can use the Feature Creature agent themselves from now on, without having to wait on me. So that's tool calls with Workers. You just saw me build a simple tool for checking feature flag status and then a more workflow-like one for toggling feature flags, where we queried workspace information, did a little validation, and then in this case blocked a feature flag toggle. LIVE: JD Vance Gives Public Update On Efforts To Root Out Fraud

Forbes Breaking News(5.6M)·53:10·3.9K views·2026-05-13
JD VanceVice President Andrew FergusonCommissioner, FTC Dr. OzPublic Figure
TL;DRThe Trump-Vance administration has launched an aggressive "all-of-government" crackdown on healthcare fraud, including deferring $1.34 billion in Medicaid reimbursements to California and issuing a nationwide moratorium on new hospice and home healthcare licenses.

TL;DR

The Trump-Vance administration has launched an aggressive "all-of-government" crackdown on healthcare fraud, including deferring $1.34 billion in Medicaid reimbursements to California and issuing a nationwide moratorium on new hospice and home healthcare licenses.

Significant Enforcement Actions Against States

  • California Medicaid Deferral: The federal government is deferring $1.34 billion in reimbursements to California due to gross negligence in fraud oversight.
      • Outlier Billing: Officials identified $630 million in billing from the top 5% of "egregious outliers" whose patient volume and billing rates were deemed impossible.
      • Excessive Growth: Spending on personal care services in California is growing at twice the national average, resulting in an estimated $500 million in at-risk federal funds.
  • Medicaid Fraud Control Units (MFCUs): The administration is demanding accountability from state-run fraud units that receive federal funding.
      • Funding Ultimatums: Letters were sent to all 50 states requiring proof of aggressive fraud prosecution; failure to comply will result in the federal government "turning off" funding for these units.
      • Performance Disparity: VP Vance highlighted that Hawaii has had zero fraud convictions in recent years, and New York had only nine indictments despite a $100 billion program. In contrast, Indiana—with a third of New York’s population—had four times as many indictments.

Reform of Hospice and Home Healthcare Systems

  • Mass Suspensions in Los Angeles: Dr. Oz announced the suspension of 800 fraudulent hospices in the Los Angeles area, which accounted for $1.4 billion in billing last year.
      • Lack of Appeal: Despite the mass shut-down, fewer than 20 of the 800 entities have contacted the government to claim they are legitimate.
  • National Licensing Moratorium: Effective immediately, there is a nationwide moratorium on all new hospice and home healthcare licenses.
      • Preventing Migration: The move is intended to stop "ballooning," where fraudsters shut down in one state (like California) and immediately reopen in neighboring states like Nevada or Arizona.
  • Identity Theft Protections: The task force is addressing "stolen identities" of beneficiaries. Examples include fraudsters signing healthy seniors up for hospice care—thereby revoking their standard Medicare benefits—and draining EBT cards from low-income families.

New Administrative Tools and "War Rooms"

  • The Medicaid War Room: Transitioning from a "pay and chase" model to real-time prevention.
      • Real-Time Auditing: A new virtual "war room" utilizes data analysts, forensic auditors, and law enforcement to monitor claims before they are paid.
      • Proven Results: Kim Brandt noted that the existing Medicare War Room has stopped over $2 billion from being paid to high-risk providers since March 31 of last year.
  • Inter-Agency Collaboration: The task force creates a "powerful fist" by merging the authorities of CMS, HHS-OIG, the DOJ, the FBI, and the Treasury.

Economic and National Security Context

  • Budgetary Impact: VP Vance argued that rooting out fraud could save hundreds of billions of dollars, potentially extending the solvency of the Medicare trust fund for future generations.
  • Foreign Policy and Iran: Regarding the conflict with Iran, Vance emphasized a "red line" focused on preventing nuclear proliferation through diplomatic and military options, while acknowledging domestic inflation concerns.
  • Security Infrastructure: Vance defended a $1 billion security and infrastructure project at the White House, stating the cost is driven by essential protective measures for the President and the line of succession.

Notable Quotes

"The first victim [of fraud] is the American taxpayer... but it also has as its victims the people who are meant to benefit from these programs, who are not able to benefit... because of what a disaster some of the fraud has wrought." — JD Vance, Vice President of the United States
"We are a large rhino that can be stabbed effortlessly by foreign governments, syndicated criminal entities, and smaller-time operators who can take advantage of the system with as little as a Medicare beneficiary number." — Dr. Mehmet Oz, CMS Official
"When the states just take this money and turn it into a jobs program for blue state lawyers, they are effectively participating in the elder abuse in those states... If the states won't protect American citizens, the President and the Vice President will." — Andrew Ferguson, Executive Director of the Anti-Fraud Task Force
"We were looking at the fraud and abuse issue and saw that it was all 'pay and chase.' We were going after the money after it was already out the door. We thought there surely has to be a better way." — Kim Brandt, HHS Official
Good afternoon, everybody. How are we doing? Good. It's good to see you all. So as you know, the President just landed in China a few hours ago. I always, you know, you may know that because of Secret Service protocols, I don't travel outside of the country with the President of the United States. On days like today, I sometimes feel like Macaulay Culkin in Home Alone. I walk into the White House and it's very quiet and no one's there. It takes me a second to realize exactly what's going on. But we've got a very big announcement today, actually a number of big announcements, and I'm looking forward to hearing from Andrew and Dr. Oz and Kim about that. Let me just say that we've been hard at work over the last several months trying to make sure that we take care of the people's money. We are trying to make sure that the services that exist for the betterment of our fellow citizens are protected for the people who actually need them. Whether they're low-income kids who need access to food resources or low-income families who need access to medical benefits, those programs should exist for the American people for whom Congress has said they're entitled. They don't exist to enrich fraudsters. And that's exactly what this fraud task force is about. One of the things that I thought going into it, but that I've learned about fraud, is that it always has two fundamental victims. The first victim is the American taxpayer who has generously paid into these programs in an effort to give their fellow citizens a leg up. But it also has as its victims the people who are meant to benefit from these programs, who are not able to benefit or maybe are benefiting less because of what a disaster some of the fraud has wrought in these programs. Let me just give you a few concrete examples of what we're worried about, of what we're targeting, and also what we're going to do to solve some of these problems. So, first, we're announcing that the federal government is deferring 1.3 billion dollars in Medicaid reimbursements from the State of California. The simple reason is because the State of California has not taken fraud very seriously and because of that we see a lot of the consequences that I just referenced. And again, let me highlight the fact that this really involves two separate victims. There are California taxpayers and American taxpayers who are being defrauded because California isn't taking its program seriously. But also, you have people who've been prescribed medications that they don't even need. Sometimes they've had drugs put into their bodies that they don't need because fraudsters have actually encouraged false prescriptions and false administration of medications. So think about this: you're just a person trying to go see a doctor, and you assume that your doctor is doing the right thing. But these fraudulent healthcare providers are getting rich by giving people medications they don't even need. It's a defrauding of the American taxpayer, but it's also a violation of the trust that should exist between every American and the people who prescribe them medications. That's one thing that we're targeting, and this is why we're taking this action. We want California to get serious about this fraud. The second thing I want to talk about requires a little bit of background explanation. We're going to very aggressively encourage the states to take Medicare fraud more seriously. I think a lot of you understand, but for those of you watching at home who don't appreciate this: the Medicaid system that exists in the United States is really 50 separate systems. The federal government pays most of the Medicaid money, but then each of the individual states actually administers the Medicaid program. Now, we have red states and blue states that go after fraud aggressively, but we also unfortunately have some states—mostly blue states unfortunately—that do not take Medicaid fraud very seriously. So you have what are called Medicaid Fraud Control Units, or MFCUs. These anti-fraud units within the Medicaid program are generously funded. The federal government gives these fraud control units many, many billions of dollars across the United States of America so that they can take Medicaid fraud seriously. But let me just give you an example to highlight how absurd some of these Medicaid fraud programs are because they're not actually being used at all. This is a tool. This is a tool that we want the states to use, but unfortunately a lot of states aren't using these tools at all. Let me highlight this with just a couple of separate statistics. The Hawaii Medicaid program has received billions and billions of dollars from the federal taxpayers, and of course, Hawaii taxpayers have paid a lot of money into this program over the last years as well. Guess how many convictions or indictments Hawaii has had over the last few years in its Medicaid fraud program? The answer is zero. Not a single indictment, not a single conviction, because the administrators of the Hawaii program just don't take it seriously. They don't think that fraud is a big enough problem. They don't care about protecting resources, and they don't care about protecting that Medicaid program. So what does that mean? That means that if you're committing fraud in Medicaid in Hawaii, at least up until now—hopefully now they're going to take it seriously—you have had effectively free rein from the government of Hawaii to commit as much fraud as you want. That is a complete disgrace. In New York, which does a little bit better but not by much, New York has had nine indictments over the last year. Nine indictments. That's a 100 billion dollar Medicaid program just in New York and you've had nine indictments. Indiana, which has about a third of the population of the State of New York, has had more than four times as many indictments over the same period. So do we think, does anybody here seriously think that the good people of Indiana are 12 times more likely to commit fraud than the people of New York? No, of course not. That's absurd. What is happening is that the leadership in New York is just not taking the fraud issue seriously. They're not using these anti-fraud control units to actually investigate and indict the fraud. So what are we going to do to fix that? Today we are sending across 50 Medicaid programs letters that will require them to show that they are effectively and aggressively prosecuting Medicaid fraud in their states. And if they do not aggressively prosecute Medicaid fraud, we are going to turn off the money that goes to these anti-fraud units. I would say a couple things about this. You may think obviously this is a Republican administration and we're all proud Republicans up here, so you may think that this is purely a red state or blue state issue. That's actually not true. We see Medicaid fraud issues in Ohio, the state that I used to represent in the United States Senate. We also see Medicaid fraud issues in a state like Maryland, which is obviously a very blue state. But both Ohio and Maryland have worked with us to take this issue seriously. They've worked with the guys behind me. They've worked with our entire team to make sure that they take fraud seriously. Again, this doesn't have to be a red state or a blue state issue. This is just basic good government. However, states like California, states like Hawaii, and states like New York have completely not taken the fraud issue seriously in the Medicaid program. And so for those states that refuse to get serious about fraud, we're going to turn off that anti-fraud money. If we continue to find problems, we can turn off other resources within their state Medicaid programs as well. Our goal here is not to do that. We don't want to turn off any money. What we want to do is ensure that people are taking fraud seriously. We want to protect Medicaid. We want to protect Medicare. But we can't do that if the states that are administering those programs are allowing those programs to be fleeced by fraudsters. So we encourage, whether it's California or New York or Maryland or Ohio, we encourage people to work with us. We want to help you use technology and other tools to get rid of the fraud, to get to the root of the fraud. We want to help you. But we can only help these state programs if those state programs are willing to help themselves. So these letters are the first step, the first effort to try to force these states to get serious about prosecuting fraud, and that's exactly what we're doing. Let me just talk about one final issue here. We see a lot of fraud in the home healthcare and in the hospice care system. I think Dr. Oz will probably talk a little bit more about that but again, you have to appreciate that this fraud issue is fundamentally theft from the taxpayer but also theft from people who need these programs. Just last month a California psychotherapist, a woman who had spent 40 years treating patients and helping people deal with problems, found out that her Medicare program had been turned off and she did not have access to the resources that she had been paying into for her entire life. Why? Because a fraudster had stolen her identity and signed her up for healthcare services that she didn't need, and so she had been turned off of the healthcare services that she did need. This happens way too much in the United States of America, and it happens because until recently we did not have a government or an administration that actually took the fraud program and took anti-fraud prevention seriously. So let me just close with one final observation here. First of all, why are we doing this now? Why weren't we doing this two years ago? And the simple answer is because we didn't have a President and we didn't have an administration who took fraud seriously. So I want to thank the President of the United States, though I know he's busy in China. All of these efforts, all of these things that we're finding, are because we have a President who has told us: take the fraud issue seriously, get to the root of it, and turn off the money when we find out that money is going to fraudsters. So that's exactly what we're doing. But I just want to say that for me, and I think for a lot of people who are involved in this effort, this is very personal. I'm not just the Vice President of the United States; I come from a family and I grew up in a family where we sometimes benefited from these programs. The generosity of the American people is one of the most amazing things about our country. I think all of us of course just passed tax day. None of us like paying our taxes, but we do it. And why do we do it? Because we know that it goes to give a bite to a low-income family that can't afford food. We know that it goes to pay the military who keep us safe. We know that it goes to some essential services. But that trust—that trust in our government and that trust in our institutions—it depends on us taking that trust seriously. How long are people going to pay into programs if they know that that money doesn't go to a low-income kid who needs healthcare, but instead goes into a fraudster getting rich? This doesn't work and this doesn't make sense unless we take fraud seriously. I'm proud to be part of an effort in our government, and I'm proud to lead the effort in our government to finally take fraud seriously. We want to protect these programs for the kids and the families who need them. We want to ensure that the American taxpayer isn't getting fleeced. And I'd say just a few months into it, gentlemen, we have done a very, very good job. Let's keep it up. So let me turn it over now to Andrew Ferguson, who's the executive director of our anti-fraud task force. Andrew has done an amazing job, but I have to give a special shout out—not just because she's my fellow Ohioan—to Kim, who has probably found more fraud and has saved the American taxpayer more money than any single official within the government over the last three months. Kim, you have been an all-star. We're proud to have you on the team. Thank you, guys. Well, I couldn't have put it better than the Vice President did. The only reason that what happened today happened is because President Donald Trump took this issue seriously. This is an issue that has plagued this country for decades, and he is committed to an all-of-government approach. So to him is to whom the credit goes. But the Vice President deserves tremendous credit here. It's hard to get the whole machinery of government moving. You need someone with the Vice President's abilities, with his gravitas, and with his determination to drive at these issues. That's the other reason we're here today. And with all due respect to the press, with this room full of reporters, it should not have taken a brave kid with a cell phone camera to point the American people at this issue. This is the result of decades of corruption, particularly in blue states like Hawaii, as the Vice President was just explaining, California, Minnesota, and New York. These states have taken tens of billions of dollars to pay lawyers to go after Medicaid fraud but just simply have not done it at all. That's the whole point of these programs. If we have these cooperative federalism programs—and we love federalism, we want to partner with states, we love subsidiarity—but if the states are going to participate in this, they have to protect their own citizens when they get access to these dollars. And not only are these dollars supposed to go to fight Medicaid fraud, I want to shine a light on the real human dimension of the complete negligence and malfeasance of some of these states here. One of the things they are obliged by law to do is stop elder abuse. That is one of the express commands that Medicaid fraud control units are given: you are supposed to police Medicaid abuse and elder abuse in the Medicare system too, where so many of America's elderly citizens receive care. And so when the states just take this money and turn it into a jobs program for blue state lawyers, they are effectively participating in the elder abuse in those states. This is because the American people turned over their money to these state programs to protect their parents and their grandparents from elder abuse. If you aren't doing it, you are effectively participating in that process. The Vice President highlighted this problem that we have in Medicaid, which is that if a fraudster takes your identity and signs you up for benefits, when you go to try to get the actual benefits you need, you can be denied them. This is a problem we have found all over the federal government in benefits programs. I mean imagine—we have examples of this—a mother on SNAP benefits going to the grocery store and trying to scan her EBT card to get groceries for her children, but some gang somewhere in California took her identity and maxed out her EBT card. Her children are going to go hungry because of fraud that no one in the states is policing. And that is why what we are doing today is so important. The states should be our partners with this. We want to partner with the states, but what we aren't going to do as stewards of the American taxpayer dollar is to send those dollars—billions of them—to the states to line the pockets of state attorneys general who don't want to participate in protecting our benefits programs. The money needs to reach the people who really need it, the people that the American people have agreed to part with their hard-earned dollars to help. And if the states don't want to cooperate, for the first time HHS, the fraud task force, the Vice President, and the rest of the federal government are going to tell you: cooperate or lose your funds. We will do the job for you because at the end of the day, if the states won't protect American citizens, the President of the United States and the Vice President will. I am so honored to be able to participate in this process because this is ultimately about making sure not only that we aren't stolen from as taxpayers, but that the American people who are entitled to this for Medicaid and for Medicare—people who have paid into these programs their entire lives—actually get the benefits that they are entitled to. And with that, I'm going to turn it over to Dr. Oz to talk about all of the efforts at HHS. Thank you, Andrew. Mr. Vice President, thank you for the leadership and the spirited desire to harness a group of stallions. They're back there by the way, if you want to see the men and women who are leading the task force. They're at the back of the room, but they've done all the work that allowed us to come up here. In February, we had the largest anti-fraud announcement from CMS the day after the State of the Union address, in which the President created this task force and appointed the Vice President to lead it. Today's effort is larger. It's much larger. And there's reason for that. Half of the fraud we believe in the federal government could be coming out of healthcare services. We are a large rhino that can be stabbed effortlessly by foreign governments, syndicated criminal entities, and smaller-time operators who can take advantage of the system with, as Andrew pointed out, as little as a Medicare beneficiary number, which is basically a credit card to defraud this system. Unfortunately, that results in about 100 billion dollars of estimated theft just from Medicare and Medicaid. To put it in the context of affordability, which is a very important subtext of this discussion, we would be able to double the life expectancy of the Medicare trust fund if we could deal with the fraud issues just in Medicare. Anyone who's out there working their tail off hoping Medicare will be there for them—and we know many young Americans don't think that's going to be the case—it will be the case if we take the fraud out. It'll be there for you and your children as well. Stopping this as a means of extending the Medicare trust fund is a noble purpose, but as has been pointed out by the Vice President and Mr. Ferguson, it is about the human cost. If these fraudsters are willing to take your money, they will definitely steal your life and take your health along the way. So let's transition to the five major actions. I'm going to go through them in order. I'll share them with Kim whose nickname is Killer Kim. Mr. Vice President, you'll know why in a second. She is unstoppable and you want federal employees like Kim out there like bulldogs going after this fraud. It's right there for us to find, and if we have partnership with the states, it'll help a lot. First, the California Medicaid deferral that the Vice President mentioned is 1.34 billion dollars. Their submissions of Medicare records and the backup estimates are generating major red flags for us. We have looked carefully at these records. We are allowed to audit looking back for a quarter. Dan Brillman and Caprice Knapp, who lead the Medicaid program, spearheaded this effort with a great team. We've discovered 630 million dollars in billing from the folks who are egregiously the top 5% of outliers in billing. These are numbers so big you cannot imagine anyone billing for these numbers of patients and that much for each patient. And so we're asking California to clarify for us how it got there. The second big area of observation is that when you bill within a state, you should be comparable to the states around you. But in California, that's not been the case. In California, the growth of spending in personal care services and in home services is twice the rate of the average of the rest of the country. Let me say that again. The rest of the country is at one number, but California is twice that number in its growth rate. We estimate there's 500 million dollars that could be at risk taken from federal taxpayers. That means people in New Mexico, which is a blue state, are paying extra taxes so that California can get away with something that they can't defend. So there's 500 million dollars there we're going to defer until they can show us more convincingly why that's the case. There's another 200 million dollars that we're stopping in questionable expenditures. Much of that is linked to immigration-related costs, for instance, folks who are undocumented and we're not sure why we're paying for this. As you know from the Working Families Tax Cut legislation, which I'm going to emphasize is a program that saved Medicaid, the President and Congress saved Medicaid with that bill. There's another 200 million dollars at risk there. Add it together: 1.34 billion dollars. It's the largest deferral we've ever made. We're making it for a good reason. We'd like the state to at least come to the table and explain to us how these outlier payments have been generated. The second major issue is home healthcare fraud and hospice fraud. So let's just clear this up. Hospice is for the end of life, designed 40 years ago as a beautiful program intended to take care of you because you have cancer and you're not going to survive more than six months. To do that, you trade your Medicare benefit for a hospice benefit. You give up Medicare because why would you want to have coverage for things when you are dying and you want to die with dignity and grace? It's a beautiful program. Unfortunately, in California and other states, fraudsters have stolen that Medicare benefit and given you hospice you didn't want. This means you can't go to the ER, you're not getting care, and you can't even find out what happened because you weren't in the mix of this. There have been a lot of people complaining about this over the years, including people that should have been listened to like the Auditor General of California, who in 2022 raised this issue to the current Governor. For some reason, they have not taken action. Unfortunately, we have a reality that again we've got to face: a third of all hospices in the entire country are in Los Angeles. Ask yourself how is that possible? It's not. There are not that many people dying in Los Angeles. We're not talking about California, just Los Angeles. So Kim and the team, along with Jeanine and a bunch of other great folks, went through the list of all the people and we believe that at least half of the hospices in the entire area around Los Angeles are fraudulent. Today we announce 800 of those hospices have been suspended. That's getting us close to where we think we should be. 800 hospices that last year charged the federal taxpayer 1.4 billion dollars will no longer be paid. Now Mr. Vice President, if your core business was shut down by the federal government, would you call? Yes, you would call. But it's crickets. We have fewer than 20 who have complained, and we're not even sure those are legitimate. 800 hospices shut down and we're not hearing much; it's like crickets out there. So we believe we're on the right path here. Next is a nationwide moratorium on all new hospice and home healthcare. Why is that possible? Because when we squeeze the balloon in California and slow their growth of hospice and take away the payments to 800 hospices, we know what those fraudsters do. They move to nearby Nevada. Next door, Nevada has a seven-fold increase in hospice already. Arizona and Texas—they're moving across the country. So we're today announcing a moratorium nationally. There will be no new hospices or home healthcare opened in this country. If you have the program now, you can keep it. You can go to ones that already exist. We're not taking away any services. However, there will be no new licenses granted until we can figure out a better way of working across government. Let me highlight this because what Andrew is leading and what the Vice President is chairing with this task force is very simple. You have CMS, you have OIG with March Bell sitting over there, you have the Department of Justice, you have the FBI, and you have Treasury. You put those things together and you make a powerful fist. And that's the fist we're going to punch through bureaucracies and realities that are stopping our ability to protect the American people. I'm going to leave you one last thought and then I'm going to ask Kim to cover some of the issues around the MFCUs and the Medicaid war room that she's creating. It is a reality that states are all over the place in their desire to partner with the Vice President in this task force. There was a letter sent out two weeks ago asking every single state to respond within 10 days as to whether or not they would be willing to revalidate their providers. What that means is checking people providing home healthcare services which are unlicensed or unverifiable right now. Basically, you're having your kids carry the groceries upstairs for you. These are services your family used to provide for you. We asked all the states to work with us to validate if these are legitimate services from legitimate providers. Every single state responded. So contrary to the narrative around red and blue, every state wants to protect their citizens. Some are just more competent or brave enough to actually make those steps. And for some reasons, as you heard today, California has been lax in this endeavor. We hope this wake-up call will be heard loudly across the beautiful State of California whose people we're trying to protect, sometimes from their own leaders. Kim. So when we took office a little over a year ago, we were looking at the fraud and abuse issue and saw that it was all "pay and chase." We were going after the money after it was already out the door. We thought there surely has to be a better way. So we created the Medicare Fraud War Room where we looked at claims in real time before they were paid. This allowed us to determine whether they were legitimate or if they were the outliers that we've heard examples of today. How could we stop it? Over the past year since the Medicare War Room started on March 31st of last year, we have stopped over 2 billion dollars from going out the door just by targeting those high-risk providers. Recently, a couple of weeks ago, we decided to take it a step further and we're now doing a Medicaid War Room. What's unique about this is it's the first time that we've ever partnered across the board with our law enforcement partners, the HHS OIG, DOJ, the states, and the MFCUs—the Medicaid Fraud Control Units—all working together for the first time to look at Medicaid claims in real time. We can now determine how we can stop those payments from going out the door. We want to stop the instances of an 89-year-old woman who had over 5,000 skin substitutes or to stop the types of things that are just really aberrantly ridiculous. What we've done is we have data analysts, we have forensic auditors, and we have law enforcement people all coming together in a virtual room. It's not an actual room like you think of on TV, but they're all in a virtual room where they are looking at this in real time and then making determinations about how we can shut those bad guys down and how we can take action. What has happened is we are now using our administrative authorities—payment suspensions, overpayments, civil monetary penalties, and the HHS OIG exclusion authority—to really be able to take action against these people. Hopefully, we can bring that money back where it needs to go and never let it go out the door. The key is we want to keep it so that it can provide the services for the people who most need it and who most benefit from it, and stop the bad actors from getting all the benefit. Thank you. Thank you, Kim. So Dr. Oz, I want to make sure I understand this well. So you're saying that we kicked 800 fraudulent healthcare providers off of the Medicare system and not a single one of them called the government and said, "Hey, you made a mistake?" We've had a handful of calls. We're not sure they're legitimate yet, but it's fewer than 20 out of 800 and we're auditing them. Unbelievable. So at least 780 are not even trying to claim that they're not fraudulent. And again, those are businesses that we were giving hundreds of millions, in some cases billions of dollars to, not to provide services but to make a fraudster rich. It's just completely insane. We think we're going to be much more effective if we're working with California as opposed to fighting California over every billion dollars of Medicaid fraud that exists in their system. I mean, there could be tens of billions, hundreds of billions of fraudulent payments within the California system if you count what's going to illegal aliens, if you count what's going to fraudulent businesses, if you count what's not going to the people who actually need it but going to fraudsters. We would find that money a lot easier if California was working with us, which is why we've taken some of these steps. The letters fundamentally are outreach. We're going to the states and saying: here are the things that we can do to work on this issue together. And of course if they don't work with us, there are going to be penalties. We don't want to get there. We want to work with these guys. You heard Dr. Oz say this earlier. I've looked at the numbers myself, and it's true. If you just deal with the fraud issue, you can prolong the Medicare and Medicaid trust funds well into the future. So it does have a significant budgetary impact. Whether that's hundreds of billions of dollars or maybe even more, we're not going to know until we do the work, but we're going to do the work. Yeah, go ahead. Mr. Vice President, Reagan Reese with the Daily Caller. What's your reaction to the alleged home health fraud in Ohio and why do you think this is happening in a red state? And just a question of clarification: you mentioned that some states aren't cooperating. What does that look like? Are they not responding to your calls? Are they stonewalling you guys? If you could expand on that. So I'll let Dr. Oz go into the details a little bit. But on the Ohio issue in particular: look, this happens everywhere. We're a big country, and hundreds of billions of dollars go out the door every single month from the federal government. Inevitably, you're going to have people who try to take advantage of it. What bothers me is not that you have a few fraudsters out there, but that the government hasn't taken seriously pushing it back. You're always going to have bad people. You're always going to have a few bad apples try to take advantage of the system. What's so unique about this country until recently is that we didn't take that seriously. We didn't try to push back against the fraud, and that's what's changed. When I talk about cooperating with these various states—and again, we've had some good cooperation with both red states and blue states—it's very context-specific. But for example, let's say that we know we have a number of fraudulent Medicare or Medicaid providers. Working with the federal government to identify who those are allows us to kick them off the rolls so that when those fraudulent companies submit a reimbursement, they don't actually get any money in return. Now sometimes the federal government has a better perspective on that, and sometimes the states do. But that's one example of ensuring that local providers that are fraudulent are not able to get reimbursed. Dr. Oz, I'm sure you probably have a hundred more in that brain of yours. Succinctly, Minnesota—where we announced a deferment when the Vice President and I were in this room several months ago—sued us. And we're trying to get them to wake up to a problem. The amount that we deferred was a small percentage of their rainy day fund. They sued us and sought a temporary restraining order. That's not what a partner does. The judge did not give them a temporary restraining order. We're going to California now in part because we couldn't get them to respond on things that we think they should have taken proactive steps on. If they've been warned for at least four years by their own Auditor General that there's a massive problem with hospice and they don't deal with it, it pollutes the entire ecosystem. It creates corruption that rots the foundation of who we are. So on the 19th of December, after it had gone through all the different processes, the State Department of Health in California was going to mandate by the first of the year that you cannot have more than one hospice per provider. This is a red flag issue. Why would a doctor need to have 20 hospices? Have one hospice and do it right. They do 20 hospices because they're taking bribes; they're selling their license and their soul. For reasons we don't understand—and I'd love to pose that right here to the State of California—someone very senior in their government pulled down that rule, never let it issue, and we never got help from them. That's why we went out the first week of January to say: what's going on here? And we find out that as the Vice President mentioned, the Medicaid fraud detection units sometimes are not being enabled to do their jobs. These are the kinds of behaviors that people take when they're trying to hide the ball. A question I'd love to ask the media to opine amongst yourselves—and we're debating it as a task force—is: do the governors always feel that fraud in Medicaid is a flaw, or are there sometimes reasons why it might be a benefit? Might it be a feature of the way they're running Medicaid? The same way we have to stop legalized money laundering through state-directed payments and provider taxes in the Working Families Tax Cut legislation, we are concerned that some governors see these programs as jobs programs. The downstream impact of that resembles political patronage that we're very serious about. Mr. Vice President, Leonardo Feldman with Newsweek. Can you tell me a little bit about, you know, the President has been toying with this idea of a joint ticket between you and Rubio? What do you think about that? But I think both of us are very much focused on accomplishing the American people's business right now. Sorry, changes to which program in particular? The Optional Practical Training program. Yeah, so we're looking into a lot of this stuff. We're also trying to make sure that we do this in a way that's defensible and that's not going to get challenged in a court, or if it does get challenged in a court, where we can defend things. So Dr. Oz, do you want to say anything about that particular program? We're going to keep looking at everything. I will tell you that once a week I get an update from Andrew and from the team on the number of things that we've found in the anti-fraud task force. And it's kind of shocking. You know, I go on the road and talk to a lot of folks out there. I was in Iowa last week and one of the most common questions I got was: is the anti-fraud thing hard? And I'm almost confused by the question because unfortunately there's so much fraud in the federal government. Record-Bee with The Federalist. Thank you. I don't think that we have 186,000 ghosts walking into the grocery store and receiving benefits. I think that we have people who are claiming that they are getting food stamp benefits using the identity of a dead person. So it's very much a living person—it's a living fraudster who's stealing the identity of a dead American. I just want to be very clear about that. But look, we know this is happening. We think that it's costing the American people hundreds of billions of dollars. By the way, another hidden cost of fraud is that it drives up the cost of goods for everybody. Because if you've got hundreds of billions of dollars chasing food that is going to living fraudsters using dead Americans' IDs, that's going to raise costs for everybody. So it actually is one of those things that has multiple victims, including the fact that it's driving up the cost of goods for other Americans. All the way in the back. Daniel Baldwin, One America News. It's a fundamental principle of good government. It shouldn't be anything Republican or Democrat; it's just good government. Go ahead. Taylor with Spectrum, good to see you. Jennifer Jacobs, CBS News. On Iran, can you tell us where negotiations stand? The President before he left for Beijing called the latest proposal unacceptable.
