Paul Graham, Founder of Y Combinator, Live from Stockholm

Y Combinator(2.2M)·21:58·34.6K views·2026-05-13
Paul GrahamFounder, Y Combinator
TL;DRAmbitious founders must spend time in Silicon Valley to calibrate against the world’s best talent and internalize a unique "pay-it-forward" culture; Stockholm can become the Silicon Valley of Europe by encouraging founders to export themselves…
temporarily and return with...

TL;DR

Ambitious founders must spend time in Silicon Valley to calibrate against the world’s best talent and internalize a unique "pay-it-forward" culture; Stockholm can become the Silicon Valley of Europe by encouraging founders to export themselves temporarily and return with global capital, connections, and high-speed execution habits.

The Power of the "Big Center"

  • Historical Precedents: Just as Paris was the center for painting in 1870 or Hollywood for movies in 1950, Silicon Valley is the undisputed center for startups. Ambitious people must go to the center to find the highest concentration of talent.
  • The Serendipity Factor: The primary benefit of being in a hub is the frequency of unplanned, high-value meetings.
      • Unplanned vs. Planned: Unplanned meetings are often more valuable because they lack the conservative constraints of "reason-based" scheduling, allowing for outlier opportunities.
      • Selection Efficiency: Casual interactions allow founders to filter for shared interests and potential partnerships within seconds.
  • Calibration Against "Big Fish": Moving to a major pond allows founders to measure themselves against industry leaders.
      • Humanizing Success: Meeting icons like Sam Altman or Brian Chesky reveals they are not a "different species," making massive success feel like a difficult but achievable goal rather than an impossible one.

Speed and Market Dynamics

  • Decisive Investment: Silicon Valley investors move significantly faster than their European counterparts.
      • Competitive Pressure: Because the best deals "time out" quickly, investors are forced to be decisive. High valuations and rushed timelines are common complaints, yet SV investors consistently achieve better empirical returns than those in Europe.
  • The "Prophet" Paradox: Founders often gain more respect in their home country only after leaving it.
      • The Dropbox Example: Drew Houston was ignored by Boston investors until Sequoia (in Silicon Valley) showed interest; the Boston firm then faxed a term sheet with a blank valuation to win him back. It was too late.
  • Aggressive Execution: In major centers, founders "egg each other on." The proximity to competition prevents founders from "half-having" ideas and forces them to move before someone else does.

The Silicon Valley "Pay-It-Forward" Culture

  • Radical Helpfulness: A defining trait of the Valley is people helping "nobodies" for no immediate reason.
      • Cultural Evolution: This behavior evolved because today's "nobodies" frequently become tomorrow's billionaires.
      • The Ron Conway Model: High-level mentors often perform so many favors they stop "keeping score," creating an environment where there is no conservation law for favors—only an increasing supply of them.
  • Trust and Integrity: This culture operates like honesty; by helping everyone, you don't have to track social debts, allowing you to operate at a much larger scale.

Strategy for Stockholm’s Growth

  • The "Göttingen" Strategy: To thrive, Sweden should encourage founders to go to Silicon Valley and then return. This mirrors 19th-century mathematicians who went to Göttingen, Germany, to learn and brought that expertise back to their home countries.
  • Three Benefits of Returning:

1. Increased Quality: Founders return with better skills and higher standards.

2. Capital Import: Founders bring Silicon Valley investment back to the local ecosystem.

3. Culture Import: Returning founders seed the "pay-it-forward" and high-trust culture in Stockholm.

  • Y Combinator as an API: Graham describes YC as the "optimal path" for this transition, acting as a "super-valley" that concentrates the Silicon Valley experience into a few months.
  • The European Leadership Opportunity: The title of "Silicon Valley of Europe" is currently unclaimed. Stockholm’s high livability and existing talent base make it a prime candidate if it can reach a "critical mass" of founders.

Notable Quotes

"Moving to Mount Olympus clears away the fog at the top... The summit is right there—quite high actually, but it's not impossibly high or invisibly high." — Paul Graham
"In Silicon Valley, people help you for no reason... If I said that to people in Sweden, they’d think it was as strange as saying the streets are clean for no reason. You don’t need a reason to have clean streets; it’s just how it is." — Paul Graham
"Investors outside Silicon Valley implicitly assume that any local startup is second-rate. If you leave for Silicon Valley, you make this rule operate in reverse: you automatically rise in investors' opinion." — Paul Graham
"Money is not everything... Once you have kids, you'll find you care more about them than your company. Where do you want your kids to grow up? If you do well enough at transplanting Silicon Valley here, you could make Stockholm the Silicon Valley of Europe." — Paul Graham
I'm gonna talk about two questions today that many of you have probably wondered about. The first one is about you: should you go to Silicon Valley? The second one is about Sweden: what should Sweden do to thrive as a startup hub? Well, the first question is a lot older than startups. In fact, it's thousands of years old. Whenever there's something that people are working on very intensely, there's usually one place in the world that's the center of that thing. So, for painting, in 1870, it was Paris. For math, in 1900, it was Göttingen. For movies, in 1950, it was Hollywood. Everyone, every ambitious person working on those things at those times, had the same question you have, the same question startup founders have: should I go there? And the answer is the same now as it always is: yes, you should. You can go there for a bit and then come back, but you should at least go. The reason is the same reason it's worthwhile to move within one country. If you were living in a small village and you were interested in startups, obviously you'd benefit from moving to the capital, right? There'd be a lot of other people doing it, or at least somebody else doing it. Well, that doesn't change when you're moving across a dotted line on a map, right? That reasoning doesn't even know the dotted line is there. What exactly do you get when you move to the big center? The answer to that question is always the same, too: you get the best peers. And in fact, the talent pool expands in two dimensions: the people are better and there's also a lot more of them. And on top of that, they tend to cluster in certain places, and so the resulting concentration of talent is—I mean, it's really intoxicating. Honestly, during a YC batch, every dinner feels like this in this room. It's true, isn't it? Is Gustaf here? This is what it feels like in YC. Imagine like this every week for three months. So, I mean, it's actually pretty good that this feels like a YC dinner; that's a high standard to meet. It's very exciting to be in the same place as a lot of people working on the same thing as you, if you're working on something that not a lot of people are working on. Gustaf mentioned this: when he got to Silicon Valley, he felt like finally he'd met his people. But there's also a practical benefit that's much more important than the effect on your morale: you have a lot more serendipitous meetings that turn out to be valuable. I am still not sure why this is so, but serendipitous meetings seem to be enormously important. If you read biographies of people who've done great things, you constantly see examples of some serendipitous meeting that changes their whole life. I don't understand why, actually. Why do unplanned meetings seem to be so much more valuable than planned meetings? It could be because there are simply more of them, and so, of course, if there are more unplanned meetings than planned, then more of the outliers will be the unplanned kind. Or maybe—excitingly—maybe there's something especially good about unplannedness. Wouldn't that be cool? Maybe it's because planned meetings are too conservative. Maybe planned meetings lop off the outliers, just like deliberately trying to find startup ideas lops off the outliers. You have to have a reason for the meeting beforehand, and that makes you too conservative. Or maybe it's because there's more selection in unplanned meetings. If you think about how unplanned meetings happen, you can decide after the first few sentences whether you keep having the conversation or not, right? And so if the first few sentences are like, "Oh, you're interested in that too? Oh my gosh, we need to talk," right? But there's something about serendipitous meetings: if you're working on something ambitious, there's nothing in the world that's better than serendipitous meetings with people who are working on the same stuff. In one of the big centers for art or math or movies or startups, you just have more of them. Things also tend to move faster in the big centers because the people are better; they're more confident and thus more decisive. They also tend to egg one another on: they both encourage and compete with one another, and that means when someone who has a good idea doesn't sit on it. That's the problem with the people out in the villages, right? They sort of half-have these ideas. In fact, when there's some big startup 10 years later, they're like, "I thought of that," right? All over the world, in villages all over the world, there are people who half-had an idea, but they didn't do anything about it and they're like, "How come that guy's so famous and I'm not?" Right? For startup founders especially, there's a special case of this moving faster that's really, really valuable. Investors in Silicon Valley—have any of you tried to raise funding from investors in Europe? Well, let me tell you, investors in Silicon Valley decide a lot faster, not just because they're better and thus more confident. It's also because there's much more competition. An investor who decides that it would be a good idea to invest in a startup really cannot sit on that idea because the better, the more right this investor is, the more likely that opportunity is to time out, right? That's a very unusual situation where the more right you are, the less time you can wait. That's why Yuri Sagalov, who we know—Yuri Sagalov is fabulous—that's why he invested immediately on meeting Max. He thought, "You know, anybody else who meets this guy is going to invest in him, I better do it right now." So that's a very characteristic story: that's how things work in the Valley. The investors complain; investors always complain: valuations are too high, we're too rushed, all these startups raise money instantly, we have to decide right away, we don't have time to really meet them and get to know them. But you know what? It doesn't seem to hurt them empirically. Silicon Valley investors get better returns than European investors, despite having to decide so quickly, so they may grumble, but they're fine. Incidentally, another advantage of moving to the great center, even briefly, is that you'll get a lot more respect back home. As Jesus said, no one is a prophet in their own country. And there may be no group more dedicated to upholding this principle than investors outside Silicon Valley. Investors outside Silicon Valley implicitly assume that any local startup is second-rate. It's not a uniquely Swedish problem; it's the same everywhere. So, if you leave for Silicon Valley, you make this rule operate in reverse: you automatically rise in investors' opinion. I've seen this happen over and over again, and sometimes it's really funny. You don't even have to move. As soon as you announce, as soon as you tell them that you've been accepted by Y Combinator, often suddenly they're tripping over themselves to try and invest in you because they know once you go to the big city, that's it, right? Everybody's going to know about you. This happened with Dropbox in the summer of 2007. It's a very funny story. Dropbox was from Boston and this was a Boston batch, right? Well, for the past year, a big Boston VC firm had been keeping a benevolent eye on them, offering them lots of encouragement and advice—not money, just encouragement and advice, right? Well, because the Boston investors were so lame, we used to fly the Boston startups out to Silicon Valley for a second Demo Day after the Boston Demo Day so they could actually raise money. And when Dropbox went out to Silicon Valley, they came to the attention of Sequoia. Well, when the Boston VC firm found out that Sequoia was interested, their opinion of Dropbox changed so fast that they probably gave themselves whiplash. They sent Drew, the founder, a term sheet—they faxed it to him, anybody even remember what a fax machine is? They faxed him a term sheet with a blank valuation. "Let us invest." They went from not investing at all to "let us invest at any cost." But it was too late; Drew went with Sequoia. In 2018, they were the first YC company to go public. As it always happens, though, the biggest advantage of moving to one of these centers is not what it does for you, but what it does to you. When you're a big fish in a small pond, you don't even know how big a fish you are. But when you move to a big pond, to the big pond—you know, this whole point is now kind of obviated because you've now met Max, but I'll tell you what I was going to say anyway. When you move to a big pond, you can measure yourself up against known big fish, and you can see how big a fish you are. Surprisingly often, the news is good. Surprisingly often, you'll see Brian Chesky or Sam Altman, right, and think to yourself, "Alright, this guy is, yeah, really impressive. But he's not a different species from me. I could do what he did if I worked that hard." It's so funny, you've just had this happen right here in front of you with Max, didn't you? Because honestly, he's pretty much as big a fish as they get. He's a Swedish fish. I can't believe I got to say that. I didn't plan that. You know, honestly, if Max is still here, that may be your nickname from now on. I mean, that's so sticky, I've seen some nicknames happen, you're the Swedish fish. So that's what happens in Silicon Valley. You go there and you see these people and you think, "Okay, I could do that." But you notice how hard he works? That's what you have to do. He's an illustration both that you can do it, but also what you'd have to do in order to do it, right? So I'm going to repeat this, it's the most important part: it's not just that, "Okay, I could be like that guy." I could be like that guy if I worked as hard. Startups are really hard. So when you see someone like that, it sets a new standard that you have to meet. You don't see people like this and think, "Oh, I'm as good as that." I mean, not unless you're insane, right? What you see, what you think is, "I could be like that." It no longer seems an impossible goal, just a hard one. And for an ambitious person, there is nothing better than a high but definite threshold. I had this very metaphorical paragraph in here that I have crossed out because it seems like it's a little too bullshit, but I'll say it anyway. Moving to Mount Olympus—oh, it's an ancient Greek metaphor here—moving to Mount Olympus clears away the fog at the top of it. The summit is right there, right there, quite high actually, but it's not impossibly high, invisibly high. I'm not saying it's always this way: there must be some people who see these big fish, these Swedish fish, and think, "Whoa, I could never do what they do." In fact, you know, honestly, I was half-thinking that, calling up all these law firms. But for some reason, this doesn't happen nearly as often as it should statistically. Part of the reason for that, I think, is that when the big fish come and speak at a YC batch, they're not trying to frighten you with their formidableness. They're there to encourage you. They try to seem a little accessible. As Max gets a little more practice, he'll get better at seeming accessible. At the moment, he's just raw terrifying. But in a sense, this is not as misleading as it sounds, because it's evidence of another of the great advantages of Silicon Valley. In Silicon Valley, people help you for no reason. In fact, if I said that sentence to people in Silicon Valley, it would sound kind of strange. It would sound as strange as if I said to people in Sweden, "In Sweden, the streets are really clean for no reason." You think you have to have a reason to have clean streets, right? Well, in Silicon Valley, people would be similarly surprised: "You have to have a reason to help people?" But in most of the rest of the world, it seems like you do. I don't usually invest in non-YC companies, but I've invested in a couple of companies started by people in England that I know, and one of them just moved to Silicon Valley. And I asked them, "How was it different than you expected?" By the way, that's the best question you can ask about anybody who does or goes to any new thing: not "what was it like," but "how is it different than you expected?" And they said the most surprising thing was how helpful everyone was. I mean, they were joking about it, how like every conversation they had seemed to end with, "What can I do to help you?" "Wasn't it like that in England?" I asked, and they just laughed—no, apparently not. And that's surprising, because people in England are much more polite than people in America. I mean, even before Trump, this was true. So clearly this is something different from ordinary politeness: there's a pay-it-forward culture in Silicon Valley that's different from anywhere else I've been in the world, maybe anywhere else in the world at all. And I can understand exactly how it evolved: Silicon Valley is the place where people go from being nobodies to billionaires faster than anywhere else in the world. And so, someone who had a taste for being really nice to nobodies—you know, the kind of person who sees the nobody walking along on the street and just wants to pet it on the head as opposed to like kicking it out of the way, right?—someone like that is going to end up with really powerful friends, maybe rich too, probably rich. But if this helping nobodies was ever calculated, it's not anymore. The custom's 60 years old now. Now it just seems to people in Silicon Valley that's how everybody acts, that's the way you behave: you just help everybody all the time. Ron Conway is probably the most perfect example of this phenomenon. All he does, all day long, is help people. He doesn't even keep track of whether they're his own portfolio companies. He doesn't even remember most of the favors he does for people, and this allows him to operate on a much larger scale. It's like being honest: you don't have to keep track of all the lies you've told so you don't contradict them. You just like do favors left and right and don't even keep track of whether you're ahead or behind. When you have multiple people doing this, as you do in the Valley, there's no longer a conservation law for favors: there's just more favors. So like Ron and I have been doing one another favors for the last 20 years. By this point, I don't know who's ahead anymore, so I have to assume it's him, right? So anything he wants, I do, you know. This will happen to you too. Weirdly enough, one of the most subtle ways moving to Silicon Valley will change you is it will make you more helpful to other people, which leads to my second question: how to help Stockholm thrive as a startup hub. And here I have a surprise: the reason I chose to talk about these two questions at the same time is that they have much the same answer. So the answer to the first one, remember, is yes, you should go. You can go there for a bit and then come back, but you should go. Well, the answer to the second question is in there, in the answer to the first one. It's: you should go for a bit and then come back. Because if you do that, as long as you do actually come back, that's one of the best things you can do to help Sweden, to help Stockholm thrive as a startup hub. And if it sounds at all controversial to think that you could help Sweden by going to America, think about how it was with the previous centers. Would it have helped 19th-century mathematicians if they boycotted Göttingen, if they said, "Oh no, we have to stay and develop Swedish mathematics?" No, of course not. And in fact, they didn't. And a good thing too: the government at the time gave them fellowships that were conditional on them leaving and going to other countries and studying the math there. If you go to Silicon Valley and then come back, you're helping Sweden in three ways: you make your own startup better, so the average quality of startups here is increased; you're probably going to bring money back with you from Silicon Valley investors; and you're importing Silicon Valley culture. That culture has evolved for decades to be optimal for startups and in fact is pretty compatible with Swedish culture. It's true it doesn't have the "tall poppies" thing, which you should probably lose anyway, but it does have the high trust part, right? I know, I know it will sound like I'm talking my own book at this point, but honestly, this is so true I have to say it: surely the optimal way to go to Silicon Valley for a bit and then come back is to do YC. YC is deliberately designed to concentrate all the things that are distinctive about Silicon Valley. It's like a little super-valley within the Valley. The density of startup founders—I mean, it's ridiculous: everyone around you is a startup founder. You have instant colleagues; they're even more committed to helping you than usual for the Valley because that's one of YC's principles. Even the speed at which investors have to decide is increased, like honestly, down to the minute. And you can do it all in like four to six months. If the Swedish government designed a program to help Swedish founders experience Silicon Valley, they couldn't do better than this. And it doesn't even cost them anything, because it's funded by Silicon Valley investors. If YC were Swedish government policy, it would seem a miracle of effectiveness, to get results like that at zero cost. And they don't even have to license it; they can just call our API. There's no sales process. Of course, for it to work, the startups actually have to come back. And there is some slightly bad news in this department. YC now has a lot of data about this, and the startups that go home after YC don't do as well as the ones that stay. Startups that go back home after YC are only about half as likely to become unicorns. But there are three reasons you shouldn't be too discouraged by that. First of all, there's a lot of selection bias in this: it's not just measuring the effect that Silicon Valley has on startups, it's measuring also the confidence and the determination of the founders, right? If you're more confident and determined, you're more likely to move to another country to do your startup. Also, it's not measuring the effect of Silicon Valley on the company's performance, just their valuation. And everybody knows companies in the Bay Area can raise money at higher valuations. And third, even if you only do half as well, that's still pretty good. If you'd have become a billionaire in the Valley, so that you only have 500 million—well, there's no difference between those two things. In fact, in Swedish, it sounds even better: you have five billion kronor, right? You're a billionaire in Sweden. Money is not everything. I mean, has an investor ever gotten up in front of an audience and said that? But it's true. Money isn't everything. Once you have kids, you'll find you care more about them than your company. Where do you want your kids to grow up? Eventually, that becomes the most important question. If you do well enough at transplanting Silicon Valley here, you could do more than just make Stockholm thrive as a startup hub. Here is the exciting idea: you could make it the Silicon Valley of Europe. That job is still up for grabs. If you ask people where's the Silicon Valley of Europe, it's not like everyone immediately says there's a certain answer. I mean, if there were an answer, it would seem a ridiculous question to ask. Nobody asks like, "Where's the Silicon Valley of America?" Right? Duh. So that means the job is still available. It may seem a bit of a stretch. You might be thinking, "Well, Stockholm is very nice, but you know, it's not very big or centrally located." So, here is an encouraging thought: have you ever seen Mountain View, California? Talk about not being big or centrally located. The place is still a backwater now. Imagine what it was like in 1955 when they were founding Shockley Semiconductor, right? And yet, it was the center of Silicon Valley, at least geometrically, until 2012 when that shifted to San Francisco. All you need is a place founders want to live and a critical mass of them. Stockholm, I can tell from being here, is the kind of place founders want to live. And who knows how close you are to having a critical mass? That's the thing about critical masses: you don't know till you hit it, and then—pow. So you might already be closer than you think. Thank you everybody.

Snap’s GPU-Accelerated Secret to Processing 10 Petabytes a Day | NVIDIA AI Podcast Ep. 298

NVIDIA(2.2M)·23:35·1.4K views·2026-05-13
Prudhvi VatalaHead of Engineering Platforms, Snap
TL;DRSnap achieved a 76% reduction in data processing costs by leveraging the NVIDIA cuDF plugin for Apache Spark to run 10-petabyte daily experimentation pipelines on idle inference GPUs, requiring zero changes to existing application code.
Data Volume: Snap’s experimentation...

TL;DR

Snap achieved a 76% reduction in data processing costs by leveraging the NVIDIA cuDF plugin for Apache Spark to run 10-petabyte daily experimentation pipelines on idle inference GPUs, requiring zero changes to existing application code.

Planetary-Scale Experimentation and Statistical Rigor

  • Data Volume: Snap’s experimentation platform processes over 10 petabytes of data every morning to serve nearly one billion monthly active users.
  • Strict SLAs: Results must be processed early each morning so product managers and data scientists can act on A/B test results immediately.
  • Statistical Methods: Snap uses rigorous methods to ensure data accuracy and user privacy:
      • Heterogeneous Treatment Effects: Detecting how features perform across diverse subsets of the global audience rather than just the average user.
      • Variance Reduction: Evening out distributions to ensure "power users" do not bias experiment results.
      • Sample Size Mismatch: Identifying and correcting for instances where a feature might cause users to stop using the app entirely.

GPU Acceleration with NVIDIA cuDF (Spark RAPIDS)

  • Performance Benchmarks: By integrating the NVIDIA cuDF plugin (formerly Spark RAPIDS) on Google Cloud, Snap saw significant speedups across different workload types:
      • Join-Heavy Jobs: 3x+ performance improvement.
      • Union Jobs: Approximately 2x improvement.
      • Aggregations: Over 1.5x improvement.
  • Zero-Friction Integration: The migration required zero application code changes, allowing Snap to maintain developer productivity while modernizing infrastructure.
  • Technical Tuning: Snap utilized NVIDIA Ether to provide out-of-the-box Spark tuning, ensuring consistent parameters across GPU and CPU environments.

Innovative Resource Management: The "Idle GPU" Strategy

  • Cyclical Capacity: Snap engineers identified that global inference GPU capacity sits idle between 1 a.m. and 5 a.m. PT as major markets sleep.
  • Cross-Platform Migration: To utilize this idle capacity, Snap built a new data platform to migrate batch Spark workloads from Google Cloud Dataproc to Google Kubernetes Engine (GKE).
  • Prioritization and Preemption:
      • Real-time user needs (e.g., serving Spotlight content) always supersede batch processing.
      • Snap built a "graceful fallback" mechanism: if GPU capacity is reclaimed by live users, the system automatically shifts workloads back to CPUs or Dataproc clusters.

Quantifiable Business and Technical Impact

  • Cost and Efficiency:
      • 76% reduction in total job costs.
      • 62% reduction in required compute cores.
      • 80% reduction in memory footprint.
  • Infrastructure Stability: The migration eliminated 120 terabytes of disk and memory spill from Snap’s daily pipelines, removing a major bottleneck for the engineering team.
  • Rapid Deployment: Through a three-way partnership between Snap, NVIDIA, and Google Cloud, the project moved from prototype to full production in just eight to nine months.
"We were able to cut almost about 76% of our job costs as a result of this migration... For the Spark nerds out there, we were able to cut out almost 120 terabytes of disk spill and memory spill from our pipelines—it just vanished." — Prudhvi Vatala
"One other thing with Spark Rapids I want to mention: we didn’t have to change a single thing about how we ran the jobs. That was the beauty of it. Zero code changes." — Prudhvi Vatala
"We had to acknowledge that if a user wanted to see fresh Spotlight content, it supersedes GPU need for experimentation... so preemption had to be built in." — Prudhvi Vatala

Like, as you can imagine with as many users as we have, Snap—and Snapchat in particular—is a very complex application. So you can imagine the scale at which we operate. Especially on the data processing side, my team’s experimentation platform is dealing with 10 plus petabytes each day. It’s a massive scale, right? And then we have a strict SLA in the morning because experimentation results need to be ready for developers, product managers, and data scientists to act on as early as possible so that they can take appropriate actions. For us, accelerating data processing basically means instead of throwing more and more CPUs at the problem, figuring out a way to flatten that scale curve. So in this particular scenario, it was about figuring out how to leverage GPUs for improving our workloads, making sure they run faster, cheaper, and scale linearly or sublinearly. Unlike right now, it’s definitely super linear with feature areas. So, so that’s what accelerating means. You mentioned experimentation. What does that mean? What does it look like when you’re conducting experiments at Snap? And then maybe how does that fit into—is that where the 10 petabytes of data each morning comes from? Yeah, absolutely. So this 10 petabytes of data is only about the experimentation platform. The big data across Snap is far wider, you know. Experimentation is a little bit about Snap’s product philosophy. We believe that experimentation, safety, and privacy are core pillars for our product development and iteration. Like when we’re thinking about new product areas, when we are shipping new product features to our half a billion daily active users across the globe, we need to think about how the users are receiving it. How are they responding to it? How are they using it? Whether or not this is adding value to their daily lives? And we also have to guardrail things. Like, is it regressing their performance? Is it causing their devices to slow down? We need to be very particular about protecting their experiences as well. And Prud, along those lines with the experimentation, can you talk a little bit about the importance of A/B testing? So A/B testing—the concept of randomized controlled trials—has been around for a long time, especially in the clinical fields. But with the digital revolution, it has become the mode of bringing statistical rigor to decision making at scale. That’s what A/B testing adds to us. When we are dealing with this massive user base that is diverse by nature, from all walks of life across the globe, and we are trying to delight them and bring experiences to them, we need to make sure what we’re delivering is buttoned down. Like, it’s actually really adding value the way we think it is, right? At this scale, a lot of things can happen. And that’s where having this statistical rigor grounded in holdouts and well-defined controls and statistical methods comes in. Over the years, my team has added a bunch of statistical methods to our platform. Heterogeneous treatment effects detection, for example. You may think that feature is performing well for the global audience, but it may not perform so well for a subset. So figuring out those heterogeneous effects is one thing that we focus on. And at this scale, no matter how you slice your experiments, you’re still allowing some bias to seep in. Some power users may end up on one side of the experiment rather than the other. So how do we make sure the distributions are evened out when the experiment results are read? That’s the variance reduction aspect. That’s something my team built over time. And then sometimes when we ship a feature, if people don’t like it, they might even just stop showing up. That’s the sample size mismatch problem. So we also do a bunch of that rigorously. So that’s what A/B testing brings to the table. With all of the data processing every day, what made you think that maybe some NVIDIA tech put into the stack might help things out? How did that process start and maybe you can talk about what you’ve integrated and what you’re using. Absolutely. So I’m really proud of this, I’m really proud of my team because over the years the number of users grew. Snap ballooned in terms of footprint. The number of features we shipped, like Spotlight, AR features, AR lenses, and all of the AI features we shipped in the recent past, they’ve also been adding a lot of additional dimensions to the platform. And my team was hard at work making sure we’re scaling appropriately even as all of this scale grows. They’ve done a very good job of it historically for years now, maintaining the cost flat and performance predictable, meeting the SLAs and whatnot. One thing we came across, we came across NVIDIA Spark Rapids on one of the blog posts. We saw NVIDIA is shipping this solution to speed up our PySpark workloads by anywhere from 3.6x performance versus 50% runtime. It was phenomenal on paper, right? So that’s what drew us. I’m waiting to hear—the numbers sound good, I’m waiting to hear the rest. Yeah, yeah. So we read those and we got super excited. And then our stack was—it still is—entirely Google Cloud for the experimentation platform. We loved working with them. The Google Cloud Dataproc was phenomenal. They’ve been a fantastic partner to us throughout this scaling journey. So when this news came out with Spark Rapids, we wanted to try it out. We did a bunch of benchmarking. We had to benchmark each kind of job as well. Like, taking jobs that are heavy with joins and repartitions and shuffling of data that moves data around versus jobs that are purely unioning data from various places versus jobs that are purely aggregating, like running sums and whatnot. We had to benchmark across all of them and we noticed that even on Google Dataproc with Spark Rapids, we got about 3x plus improvement for the join jobs, and about close to 2x for the union jobs, and a little over 1.5x for aggregations. That’s largely because CPUs are already good at aggregations. And then the other thing is GPUs by nature support parallelism and high bandwidth memory on the hardware itself. That made it a very good candidate for us to pursue. And so you’re running your GPU-accelerated pipelines on Google Kubernetes, is that right? Yes, yes. That has been a very interesting journey from testing out our pipelines with Dataproc GPUs to today. And one other thing with Spark Rapids I want to mention: we didn’t have to change a single thing about how we ran the jobs. That was the beauty of it. Zero code changes. So I’m into developer productivity and developer enablement. So for me, that was music to my ears. That was very impressive. So with Dataproc, which abstracts out the Spark runtime for us, and Spark Rapids, which didn’t require us to change the jobs, it was phenomenal. It went very well, so we wanted to productionize this. We were able to—at our scale, pipelines aren’t just monolithic, right? We do a bunch of sharding and then batching of work. So we were able to migrate one shard to production on Google Dataproc using 300 GPUs. The results were phenomenal. And then in the next phase, we wanted to migrate 10 shards of our total 50 plus shard architecture. And then it needed about 3,000 GPUs, which was still doable with Dataproc on-demand GPUs. Because GPU capacity is on everybody’s minds these days, right? So that was well and good but then we didn’t have a path forward after that. We kind of hit a roadblock with on-demand GPU capacity. So we had to get creative. We started looking around. We were like, where at Snap do we have GPU capacity that we can borrow? And that’s where the real insight came for us. Snap has a global audience and the Snapchatter’s behavior is cyclical during the day. People wake up, they use Snapchat, and when they go to bed, they don’t. What that meant was when some of our biggest markets went to bed, a lot of our online inference GPU capacity was sitting idle. That was somewhere between 1 a.m. and 5 a.m. Pacific. So that was our opening, our opportunity to go tackle. And that brought about its own set of complexity, right? Because an online serving stack is not built for batch data processing. They were considered fundamentally different worlds. So all the online GPUs were tied to Kubernetes and GKE. We were already on Google Cloud, so GKE wasn’t an issue for us at all. It was actually very welcome. So we had to migrate our workloads to a Kubernetes-based Spark runtime and host it on GKE so that we can leverage what the online GPUs had to offer. And for that, we had to actually build a data platform ground up. It’s one thing for my team to just use this idle capacity, but at Snap, we wanted to make sure even as the online need for GPUs increased, as our AI footprint increased, we should still have any team at Snap be able to leverage that capacity for any of their needs as available. And then we had to also acknowledge that if a user wanted to see fresh Spotlight content, it supersedes GPU need for experimentation, right? Preemption had to be built in. Yes. So if we had a sudden spike in traffic, we had to give up GPU capacity. With all of that in mind, we built out a platform ground up and then we started migrating. And we had a lot of blockers along the way and the team got really creative. It was a phenomenal journey. Amazing. And so you’re also running an accelerated Apache Spark pipeline? Yes, yes. So a lot of our pipelines at a high level are split into daily and hourly cadence. Hourly is mostly for guardrailing, like I said, because we don’t want to break users’ experience no matter what, and having that hourly feedback cycle goes a long way in doing that. Then we also have daily pipelines which serve as the statistical authority for decision making. Our first migration to GKE plus NVIDIA Spark Rapids was the hourly pipeline because speed mattered there far more. So we migrated and operationalized it, and during that process we ran into a few corner cases. If the GPU capacity wasn’t available at 11 a.m. when everybody was active on Snap, what do we do? We had to figure out how to gracefully fallback from GPUs to CPUs. And then if the shared GKE resources itself was the constraint, then we had to gracefully fallback from CPUs to Dataproc clusters. So building all of that with operational reliability in mind was also great. Looking back on it, what learnings would you share? If there’s a listener out there who’s embarking on a similar project or trying to figure out borrowing GPUs from other parts of the company, is there a big takeaway or something that surprised you? Right, right. So the direction that NVIDIA is headed in is phenomenal for these kinds of needs. NVIDIA Spark Rapids, like I said, zero code written, zero code changed to enable it. We had to figure out the image building and environment differences and whatnot. The testing cycles obviously any production workload needs to go through that rigorous rollout process, so everybody needs to pay attention to it. But this is a real possibility, you know, the NVIDIA direction. The other thing that NVIDIA offered that really helped us a lot was NVIDIA Ether. It’s another solution that gives us Spark tuning out of the box. Especially when we had this fallback mechanism in place where we had to go from GPUs to CPUs to Dataproc, the environments are different and the Spark parameters had to be different. Stanford CS153 Frontier Systems | Jensen Huang from NVIDIA on the Compute Behind Intelligence

Stanford Online(1.1M)·68:24·29.3K views·2026-05-13
Jensen HuangCEO, NVIDIA
TL;DRComputing is undergoing its first fundamental architectural shift in 60 years, moving from pre-recorded retrieval to real-time generative intelligence.
Through a "co-design" approach across the entire technology stack, NVIDIA has achieved a million-fold increase in...

TL;DR

Computing is undergoing its first fundamental architectural shift in 60 years, moving from pre-recorded retrieval to real-time generative intelligence. Through a "co-design" approach across the entire technology stack, NVIDIA has achieved a million-fold increase in performance over the last decade, enabling the transition from simple AI models to reasoning, agentic systems.

The Reinvention of Computing Architecture

  • Shift to Generative Models: Computing has historically been "pre-recorded" (retrieval-based). The new era is defined by real-time generation that is contextually relevant and responsive to human intention.
  • The Power of Co-Design: Huang defines co-design as the simultaneous optimization of chips, compilers, software frameworks, and networking.
      • Performance Scaling: While Moore’s Law historically provided 100x gains over 10 years, NVIDIA’s co-design approach yielded a 1,000,000x increase in compute capability over the last decade.
      • Breaking Abstractions: Modern computer science must move past the "siloed" approach where chip designers and software engineers work in isolation.
  • From Training to Reasoning:
      • The breakthrough of GPT wasn't just image or text generation; it was the ability to "generate thoughts" and reason step-by-step.
      • Agentic Systems: Computing is shifting from "on-demand" (time-sharing/cloud) to continuous, autonomous agents that run indefinitely to solve complex problems.

Hardware Roadmap: From Hopper to Feynman

  • Hopper (The Pre-training Era): Designed for the massive data ingestion required for foundational model training. NVIDIA bet on multibillion-dollar systems when the market for such scale was effectively zero.
  • Blackwell (The Inference/Decode Era): Optimized for high-throughput token generation.
      • Performance: Delivered a 50x speedup in inference in just two years.
      • Architecture: Utilizes the NVLink 72 rack-scale computer to provide the massive memory bandwidth required for decoding.
  • Vera Rubin (The Agent Era): Specifically architected for AI agents.
      • Focuses on low-latency tool-use and direct communication between storage and GPUs to handle "working memory."
  • Feynman (The Future Swarm): The upcoming generation designed to manage "swarms" of agents and sub-agents working in a coordinated software ecosystem.

The Strategy for Open Models and Industry Activation

  • Democratizing Domain Intelligence: NVIDIA releases open models (e.g., BioNemo for biology, Groot for robotics, NemoTron for language) to activate industries that lack the scale to build their own foundations.
  • Open Source as Security: Huang argues that "you cannot defend against a black box."
      • Cybersecurity: Transparent, open systems allow researchers to interrogate models and deploy millions of "swarm" AIs (like NemoTron Nano) to detect and surround cyber threats.
  • Language Equity: NVIDIA prioritizes open models for smaller nations (e.g., Sweden) whose languages are not high priorities for proprietary providers, ensuring digital sovereignty.

Resource Scarcity and Institutional Challenges

  • Energy Efficiency: While compute demand may increase 1,000x, market forces are now strong enough to drive sustainable energy investment without relying solely on government subsidies.
  • The "Flops" Metric: Huang dismisses "Model Flops Utilization" (MFU) as a primary success metric.
      • Intelligence per Watt: Success should be measured by tokens per watt and actual performance on "real evaluations," not just raw hardware speeds.
  • University Compute Gap: Huang argues that the lack of compute in academia is an institutional budgeting failure.
      • Recommendation: Universities must aggregate fragmented grants to build billion-dollar campus-wide supercomputers (similar to linear accelerators) rather than relying on individual departmental laptops.

Leadership Philosophy: The Necessity of Suffering

  • Resilience through Struggle: Huang pushes back on the advice to only "do what you love."
      • The 90/10 Rule: He notes that 90% of a CEO’s job is hard work and suffering; only 10% is pure passion.
      • Character Building: Resilience is a "muscle" that can only be built by enduring pain and choosing to do one's best in difficult, undesirable tasks.
"If you’re able to do computing a million times faster, everything about computing changes. That’s really the big breakthrough... it created all this infinite abundance opportunity for everybody to think about the future." — Jensen Huang
"The way you defend against super agentic systems in the future for cybersecurity is obviously not to go into a battle of who gets the better one... we’re going to have millions, billions, swarms of cheap AIs and we’re going to systematically surround [the threat]." — Jensen Huang
"I don't mind the pain and suffering. I literally suffer through 90% of my work... you have to learn how to condition yourself to want to get to a better state, no matter how hard. You don't have that character unless you've gone through it a whole bunch of times." — Jensen Huang

Basically, co-design says that back in the old days, we abstracted computing so that the people who design microprocessors design microprocessors, people who worked on compilers worked on compilers, and people who worked on languages worked on languages, and so on and so forth. You guys know that, and we actually had different fields. But the problem—and in fact, this happened at Stanford—what's the beauty of RISC? What was the beauty of the work that John Hennessy did? The beauty of it is that you have to think about compilers and microprocessor architectures harmoniously, co-designed. Otherwise, you could end up creating a microprocessor that's super, super tight and everything is maximally optimized, but unfortunately, it's hard to compile; it's not compilable. And so, they created a simpler instruction set that exposes simplicity to compilers so that compilers could do a better job generating code. It turns out a simpler machine, co-designed with a compiler, creates better performance than two systems that were optimized individually. That's very Stanford, okay? And this is a part of your heritage, as all of your and John Hennessy's trail of amazing work that's left behind. But over the course of the last 10 years, if you just allowed microprocessors to continue to scale and you just don't touch the software and you just benefit from the speedup of semiconductors or microprocessor design, at best case you would have gotten 100X. But probably because Dennard Scaling slowed down and Moore's Law largely ended, you probably got something along the lines of 10X over the course of 10 years. Now, of course, there's a lot of different ways you could solve the opaqueness. For example, you could say, "Before it does anything, you have to reason about it to me step by step." Before you do anything at all, you have to come up with a plan, you have to reason about it step by step, but you could always lie. And so, the nice thing about transparent systems is that then everybody gets to interrogate it. If you have a transparent system, an open system, then researchers get to use it. If you have a transparent system, then the way you defend against super agentic systems in the future for cybersecurity is obviously not to go into a battle of who gets the better one. You come up with some model, a model 7.0, and the only way I combat against that is I'm completely vulnerable until I come back with an 8.0 and then you got to come back with a 9.0 and we just go back and forth, driving each other nuts. Obviously that's not the smartest way to do it. The smartest way to do it is you're going to create these incredible cybersecurity systems and what we're going to do is we're going to have millions, billions, swarms of cheap AIs and we're going to systematically surround it. It's kind of a giant dome. For example, NemoTron Nano is being used for cybersecurity. And so all these cybersecurity firms take NemoTron Nano because it's so fast and so cost-effective, you can train it to detect cyber attacks and then just deploy trillions of them. Yeah. On the topic of open scaling, you know, we hung out in January and we talked about this topic, open scaling. We talked about bottlenecks. We talked about how data's one bottleneck and compute is another bottleneck. You know, there's at least one experiment that we announced that GTC together, which was the coalition scaling idea. The second one is on how to improve utilization on compute which is increasingly scarce. It came out last week that there was a memo at xAI that said their Memphis cluster pool is running at 11% MFU utilization, which I think corresponds to something like 11 billion or something of unutilized MFU flops. How can the open space, like maybe you could talk a little bit about why coalition scaling is an experiment we're trying and how do we get utilization to be better for the open ecosystem when you don't have fully integrated companies that can optimize up and down the stack? Did you guys know what MFU is? And so, F-U, you guys don't use that anymore. So, MFU is just simply wrong, okay? It's the amount of flops, basically, that you consume while doing your work: Model Flops Utilization. And so, unfortunately, with every metric, depending on what you measure, you could be measuring the wrong thing. Let me tell you why. If you ask me, do I want to be at high MFU personally or low MFU, I would like to be at low MFU all the time. And the reason for that is because I want to be so smart I'm over-provisioned for the work. Because I'm over-provisioned, I got so many flops sitting idle. The reason for that is because the way the computing works in these large scale data centers is you have flops, you have memory bandwidth, you have memory capacity, you have network capacity, and at any given point in time, something is bottlenecked. At any given point in time, something is bottlenecked. And so what you want to do is you want to over-provision on everything so that you could avoid Amdahl's Law. Otherwise, you're fighting Amdahl's Law all the time. The second thing that we did was we said, "Okay, well after training, we'll keep making training better, but the goal of AI isn't in training, the goal of AI is inference." Suno's Mikey Shulman: Everyone Can Make Music Now

Sequoia Capital(189.0K)·34:57·3.1K views·2026-05-13
Mikey ShulmanFounder and CEO, Suno Sonya HuangPartner, Sequoia Capital
TL;DRSuno is transforming music from a passive consumption medium into an "active entertainment" platform where 90% of users are creators, utilizing a raw sound-wave modeling approach that prioritizes full-song storytelling over traditional music theory constraints.
Raw Wave...

TL;DR

Suno is transforming music from a passive consumption medium into an "active entertainment" platform where 90% of users are creators, utilizing a raw sound-wave modeling approach that prioritizes full-song storytelling over traditional music theory constraints.

Technical Strategy: Modeling Sound from First Principles

  • Raw Wave Modeling: Unlike traditional systems that encode the 12-tone Western scale or specific instruments, Suno models music as raw sound waves (float-32 numbers sampled at 48kHz).
      • Eliminating Constraints: By avoiding pre-set musical rules, the model can generate "impossible" genres, such as microtonal music or unexpected blends like country with 808s and trap with sitars.
  • Autoregression over Diffusion: The team intentionally chose autoregressive modeling to prioritize the "story" and structure of a full 3-minute song over the "crisp" audio quality typically offered by diffusion-based clips.
  • Scale vs. Alignment: Shulman notes that music is not a "scale problem" in the same way Large Language Models (LLMs) are. Success relies less on compute size and more on human preference data to align models with creative tastes.

The "Creative Entertainment" Business Model

  • The Creator Flip: While traditional platforms like Spotify are designed for passive listeners, 90% of Suno’s daily users are actively creating.
  • Gaming Parallels: Shulman views Suno as closer to gaming or coding (e.g., Claude Code) than traditional music streaming. The act of creation provides fulfillment and "warm and fuzzy feelings" that passive consumption cannot.
  • Defining "Creative Entertainment": The value lies in the process of building something personal. Shulman compares it to home cooking—people enjoy the meal more because they made it, even if a professional restaurant could produce a "better" version.
  • Reframing "Slop": Shulman argues that while 99% of AI-generated music may have no interest to the general public, it is deeply meaningful to the person who made it (e.g., a song made with a child), making the "slop" label irrelevant to the user experience.

Industry Disruption vs. Collaboration

  • Record Label Partnerships: Contrary to the "disruptor" narrative, Shulman views record labels like Warner as essential cultural institutions.
      • Positive Sum Tools: Suno aims to build products that allow fans to interact with their favorite artists' catalogs in new, monetizable ways, deepening the fan-artist connection.
  • The "Coachella" Vision: Shulman predicts a "truly interactive concert" within the next 12 months where audiences synchronously participate in music-making with the performer.
  • Modernizing the Format: Shulman highlights that the digital music experience has remained stagnant for 25 years (streaming); AI represents the first major innovation in musical format since the MP3.

Future Product Roadmap

  • Social Co-creation: Future iterations will focus on making music creation social, allowing users to send "song templates" to friends to riff on asynchronously.
  • Personalized Audio: The platform recently introduced the ability for users to use their own voices in tracks, significantly increasing the emotional resonance and attachment to the output.
  • Beyond Background Music: While many AI music tools focus on "voiceless background music," Suno is doubling down on lyrical, vocal-heavy tracks because the human voice is the most effective tool for storytelling.
"In Western music there are 12 tones. If you tell the model there are 12 tones, it will only ever produce those 12 tones... for us, it was all about let's throw away everything we know about music and let's try to do this from scratch." — Mikey Shulman
"The creation is actually the entertaining bit. And that is the big step change... we are basically using technology to allow everybody to feel those warm and fuzzy feelings [of creativity]." — Mikey Shulman
"The digital musical experience has basically not changed for 25 years. We've just been streaming music for 25 years and I think music is due for a new innovation and a new format." — Mikey Shulman
